
Data‑Driven Modelling  
with Fuzzy Sets
Lotfi Zadeh introduced in 1965 the theory of fuzzy sets, in which truth 
values are modelled by numbers in the unit interval [0, 1], for tackling 
mathematically the frequently appearing in everyday life partial truths. In 
a second stage, when membership functions were reinterpreted as possibil‑
ity distributions, fuzzy sets were extensively used to embrace uncertainty 
modelling. Uncertainty is defined as the shortage of precise knowledge or 
complete information and possibility theory is devoted to the handling of 
incomplete information. Zadeh articulated the relationship between pos‑
sibility and probability, noticing that what is probable must preliminarily 
be possible. Following Zadeh’s fuzzy set, various generalizations (intuition‑
istic, neutrosophic, rough, soft sets, etc.) have been introduced enabling a 
more effective management of all types of the existing in real‑world uncer‑
tainty. This book presents recent theoretical advances and applications of 
fuzzy sets and their extensions to Science, Humanities and Education.
This book,
•	 Presents a qualitative assessment of big data in the education sector 
using linguistic Quadri partitioned single‑valued neutrosophic soft 
sets.
•	 Showcases application of n‑cylindrical fuzzy neutrosophic sets in edu‑
cation using neutrosophic affinity degree and neutrosophic similarity 
Index.
•	 Covers scientific evaluation of student academic performance using 
single‑value neutrosophic Markov chain.
•	 Illustrates multi‑granulation single‑valued neutrosophic probabilistic 
rough sets for teamwork assessment.
•	 Examines estimation of distribution algorithm based on multiple 
attribute group decision‑making to evaluate teaching quality.
It is primarily written for Senior undergraduate and graduate students and 
academic researchers in the fields of electrical engineering, electronics and 
communication engineering, computer science and engineering.

Preface
Fuzzy sets have been widely used to represent imprecise and fuzzy infor‑
mation in the real world. Although they are effective in dealing with such 
data, their capability to handle incomplete and inconsistent information is 
restricted. Recently, several extensions of fuzzy sets have attracted attention 
because of their improved versatility.
This book, entitled Data‑Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets: Embracing 
Uncertainty, explores theories and advancements in the field. It also exam‑
ines the wide‑ranging applications of various extensions of fuzzy sets the‑
ory. Additionally, the book delves into the practical applications of fuzzy 
sets in knowledge management, including evaluating student learning abili‑
ties, assessing academic performance and screening technical articles.
The information in this extensive resource is designed to be beneficial for 
mathematicians, physicists, computer experts, engineers, scholars, practi‑
tioners and students who want to enhance their knowledge of fuzzy sets 
and their practical applications in various fields.
The book contains 16 chapters that address a wide range of topics, all in 
line with the bookʼs overall vision. Each chapter provides valuable insights 
into the study of fuzzy set theory and its extensions, as well as exploring 
their applications in various fields. Here is a brief overview of the findings 
presented in the chapters:
Chapter 1 focuses on a decision‑making model that integrates fuzzy soft 
sets into FCM (Focused Decision‑Making Model integrating Fuzzy Soft 
Sets in FCM).
Chapter 2 presents a decision‑making model based on the combination 
of Fuzzy AHP (Analytical Hierarchy Process) and Fuzzy SPOTIS (Stable 
Preference Ordering Towards Ideal Solution) proposed by Nivetha Martin 
and Jegan Karuppiah.
Chapter 3 presents two characteristic examples of decision‑making prob‑
lems in a fuzzy environment by Michael Gr. Voskoglou.
Chapter 4 introduces an approach to solve multi‑criteria decision‑making 
problems under trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy multinumbers by Irfan Deli 
and Hümeyra Karadöl.

Preface  ix
Chapter 5 proposes a new type of mean aggregation operators called 
Archimedean norm operators for aggregating trapezoidal fuzzy multi‑­
numbers based on Archimedean t‑conorm and Archimedean t‑norm, pre‑
sented by Davut Kesen and Irfan Deli.
Chapter 6 provides a short review and classification of existing approaches 
and methodologies developed by researchers and scientists in the uncertain 
inventory management domain, presented by Ankit Dubey and Ranjan 
Kumar.
Chapter 7 analyses the relevance of Critical Path Method (CPM) and 
Project Evaluation and Review Techniques (PERT) in the construction field, 
proposed by M Navya Pratyusha and Ranjan Kumar.
Chapter 8 offers a critical review and classification of existing model‑
ling approaches and solution methods relevant to Linear Programming 
Problems under uncertainty, presented by Shubham Kumar Tripathi and 
Ranjan Kumar.
Chapter 9 introduces the notation of bipolar fuzzy translations of a 
­bipolar‑valued fuzzy set to Sheffer stroke MTL‑algebras and explores its 
relevant properties, proposed by Senturk et al.
Chapter 10 focuses on the classical transportation model and introduces 
the greedy heuristic Dhouib‑Matrix‑TP1 (DM‑TP1) for optimization, pre‑
sented by Souhail Dhouib and colleagues.
Chapter 11 explores the concept of cubical fuzzy information and its 
application in solving shortest path problems, proposed by Broumi et al.
Chapter 12 conducts a comprehensive study on Dodecagonal Fuzzy 
Numbers and their applications, presented by Jeromia Anthvanet and 
Rajkumar.
Chapter 13 introduces Non‑Linear Dodecagonal Fuzzy Numbers in both 
symmetric and asymmetric forms, as well as Interval Valued Non‑Linear 
Dodecagonal Fuzzy Numbers, studied by Jeromia Anthvanet and Rajkumar.
Chapter 14 presents a simplified computing procedure for solving vague 
transportation problems using vague sets, proposed by Ms. Mary Tency 
E.L and Helen M.
Chapter 15 introduces a fuzzy Ishikawa diagram as a tool for representing 
and evaluating the quality of education, proposed by Ortega‑Chávez et al.
Chapter 16 introduces a novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology for 
evaluating the logistic performance index, presented by N. Yapıcı Pehlivan.
The successful publication of this book is indebted to the skills and efforts 
of numerous individuals. First and foremost, the advisory committee provided 
invaluable guidance to the editor throughout the editing process. The con‑
tributors, with their diverse expertise, shared their valuable perspectives on 
data‑driven modelling with fuzzy sets and their applications to knowledge 
management. Additionally, the book reviewers played a crucial role by pro‑
viding constructive feedback on the book chapters, ensuring their quality and 
relevance.

x  Preface
It is our sincere hope that this book will serve as a valuable resource 
for readers, allowing them to deepen their understanding of the theories, 
advancements and practical applications of fuzzy sets and their extensions, 
particularly in the context of knowledge management. May this book 
inspire further research and innovation in the field, benefiting scholars, 
practitioners and students alike.

Chapter 1
Integrated fuzzy soft 
FCM approach in focused 
decision‑making
Priya R. and Nivetha Martin
1.1  INTRODUCTION
Fuzzy cognitive maps (FCMs) are the decision‑making tools developed by 
Kosko [1] to make optimal decisions with uncertain data. FCMs are the 
extensions of cognitive maps introduced by Robert Axelord [2]. An FCM 
is a graphical representation of the factors concerned with the decision‑­
making problem, and the causal effects between the factors are profoundly 
explored based on manual or computational methods.
The development of FCM based on manual methods requires an expert’s 
knowledge of the basics of FCM and the background of the problem. The 
choice of the experts has to be made on the grounds of competency and 
efficacy. Another kind of development of the FCM model is using compu‑
tational methods that require less human intervention. The computation 
methods comprise algorithmic approaches under automated and semi‑­
automated models.
Construction of the FCM model requires factors and their inter‑­relational 
impacts, which are represented as nodes and edges of a directed graph, 
respectively. Edge weights signify the degree of association between the fac‑
tors and are represented by 1, 0 and −1 to denote the positive, null and 
negative natures of associations, respectively. An FCM with such discrete 
weights is termed as simple, and if the degree of association belongs to [−1, 1],  
then the FCM is defined as weighted. Thus, the type of edge weight ­values 
classifies FCM.
FCMs have varied applications in several fields, especially in obtaining 
optimal decisions. Researchers have applied FCM in different domains, 
to mention a few, Markinos et al. [3] in agriculture, Papageorgiou and 
Salmeron [4] in modeling systems, Sapkota and Karwowski [5] in pre‑
diction, Napoles et al. [6] in simulation, Murungweni et al. [7] in liveli‑
hood analysis, Mendonca et  al. [8] in industrial applications and Liu 
et al. [9] in risk factor analysis. FCMs are extended to intuitionistic FCM 
(IFCM), neutrosophic FCM and plithogenic FCM. In IFCM,  the  edge 

2  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
weights are represented using intuitionistic sets. IFCMs are also widely 
applied in various decision‑making environments—Papageorgiou et al. 
[10], Iakovidis and Papageorgiou [11] in medical analysis, Hajek and 
Prochazka [12] in supplier selection, Buyukozkan and Gocer [13] in 
risk analysis, Habibollah et  al. [14] in supply chain management and 
Hajek et  al. [15] in multi‑criteria decision‑making. Another exten‑
sion of this kind is ­neutrosophic cognitive maps (NCMs) in which the 
association between the factors is determined by indeterminate values. 
Smarandache  & Vasantha Kandasamy [16] have introduced NCM. 
Researchers have applied NCM in various fields. To mention a few, Aasim 
et  al. in the situation analysis, Martin et  al. [17] in lifestyle diseases 
analysis, Kalaichelvi and Gomathy [18] and Vasantha et al. [19] in social 
problems, William et al. [20] in medical diagnosis, Al‑Subhi et al. [21] in 
project management, Ramkumar et al. [22] and Ramalingam et al. [23] 
in COVID-19 diagnosis.
Plithogenic cognitive maps (PCMs) are the recent extension of NCM, 
and it is the generalization of FCM and IFCM. Smarandache and Martin 
[24] have introduced PCM. Martin et al. [25] have also introduced induced 
PCM, plithogenic sub‑cognitive maps. PCM is also used by researchers 
to study COVID-19. The above‑stated varied types of cognitive maps dif‑
fer by their edge weight representations. Also, in all these types of cog‑
nitive maps represented in the Fuzzy, Intuitionistic, neutrosophic and 
plithogenic environments, each factor is associated with other factors, 
and the cause‑and‑effect interrelations are expressed. The factors of the 
decision‑making problem are compared with one another in a very com‑
prehensive manner. For instance, in conventional types, the causal impact 
of one factor over another is expressed in a very general manner, but the 
nature of the impact is not specified and discussed so far. It is quite very 
natural that the association between the factors has to be narrowed down 
at times of focused decision‑making. The specificity in the associations is 
very much essential to studying the real impacts of the factors. To facilitate 
focused decision‑making, a new kind of FCM with a soft sets approach 
is introduced. Soft sets are introduced by Molodtsov [26]. Fuzzy soft sets 
introduced by Atanassov et  al. [27] have several applications in making 
optimal decisions. Researchers like Cagman et al. [28], Kong et al. [29], Liu 
et al. [30], Kazancı et al. [31], Roy and Maji [32], and Sut [33] have applied 
fuzzy soft sets in various decision‑making scenarios. Fuzzy soft sets are 
also extended to intuitionistic, neutrosophic and plithogenic soft sets just 
as fuzzy cognitive maps.
This book introduces the fuzzy soft approach in fuzzy cognitive 
maps and applies it in focused decision‑making. This new approach has 
changed the representation of the connection matrix. In this new type 
of FCM, the connection matrix representing the associational impact 

Integrated fuzzy soft FCM approach in focused decision‑making  3
between the factors has space for specificity, and the nature of the impact 
is also reflected. Just as fuzzy soft sets establish the relationship between 
the elements and the attributes, in this new type of FCM with fuzzy soft 
sets approach, the associational impacts of a factor over another factor 
are discussed under various phenomena that are considered the attributes 
as in fuzzy soft sets, and also, the representations are made as in fuzzy 
soft sets.
The fuzzy soft sets integrated with FCM decision models provide space 
for narrowing down the decision‑making, whereas the earlier FCM models 
are very comprehensive and lack the space for specificity in decision‑­making. 
The advantage of the proposed decision‑making model is substantiated by a 
focused decision‑making problem on the institutional initiatives toward the 
holistic growth of the students in the post‑pandemic period. In this decision‑ 
making problem, in addition to the factors of the problem, the nature of 
the problem is also taken into consideration. Here, the problem is circum‑
scribed around holistic growth, and this is defined in five dimensions, 
namely Physical, Emotional, Social, Spiritual and Mental. The associa‑
tional impacts between the factors of the problem are analyzed concerning 
the five dimensions of holistic growth rather than considered in a general 
sense. The decision‑making is focused on the sense that the association 
between the factors of the problem is analyzed with a special focus on the 
five dimensions of holistic growth. This kind of analysis of the associational 
impacts will certainly facilitate in making focused ­decision‑making about 
the factors of the decision‑making problem. This kind of decision‑making 
model also provides opportunities to make decisions in general and particu‑
lar dimensions.
The chapter is structured into five sections. Section 1.2 presents a brief 
description of the fuzzy soft sets‑integrated FCM decision models; Section 
1.3 applies the proposed model to a focused decision‑making problem; 
Section 1.4 consists of results and discussions and Section 1.5 concludes 
the chapter.
1.2  INTEGRATED FUZZY SOFT SETS FCM
FCMs are directed graphs consisting of edges, nodes and edge weights. The 
edge weight values decide the nature of FCM. The concept, terminology 
and methodology of FCM are explained by the following example.
Let us consider a decision–making problem with four factors. The factors 
are considered as the nodes, and their association is represented as the edges 
of a directed graph. Let the factors be F1, F2, F3 and F4. The graphical 
representation of the association shall be represented from the connection 
matrix as shown in Figure 1.1.

4  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
In FCM, the fixed point is determined by the following procedure with 
the intervention of a few terms. An instantaneous vector is of the form (1 0 
0 0 0 0…0) stating the ON and OFF positions of the factors. Let us consider 
the first factor in the ON position, then the instantaneous vector takes the 
form of (1 0 0 0). This vector is passed onto the connection matrix M and 
the resulting vector will be (0 1 1 1). The vector obtained is the threshold 
by assigning 1 to the values of the vector greater than 1 and 0 to the values 
lesser than 1. The value 1 is also assigned to the corresponding ON position 
of the factor. The updated vector is (1 1 1 1). Now, the same vector is passed 
onto the connection matrix M, the resultant updated vector is (1 1 1 1)  
which is the same as the earlier obtained threshold vector. This vector is 
said to be the fixed point of this dynamical fuzzy system. The observation 
from the vector clearly states the first factor has associations with all other 
factors. From this vector, we obtain the association only in a general sense. 
The connection matrix consists of simple edge weights. The edge weights 
shall be varied by assigning weighted edge weights [−1, 1], linguistic vari‑
ables with fuzzy quantifications. In these ways, the connection matrix gets 
modified. But in all these modifications, the associational impacts between 
the factors are determined only in a general sense but not the impacts with 
specificity between the factors. To make it so, the fuzzy soft set approach

is integrated with FCM. The same problem of finding the associational 
impacts shall be discussed under three dimensions, in that case, three con‑
nection matrices will be obtained concerning each dimension and that will 
be of the form.
The values a1, a2 and a3 represent the associational impacts of C1 over 
C2 concerning the dimensions D1, D2 and D3, respectively. The ai values 
shall be crisp, fuzzy, intuitionistic, neutrosophic and linguistic. This shall 
be represented as fuzzy soft sets in the following manner. The associational 
impacts of C1 over other factors are as follows:
	
{C2/{a1,a2,a3}, C3/{a4,a5,a6}, C4/{a7,a8,a9}}
The connection matrix comprising associational impacts with specific‑
ity shall be split into three connection matrices pertaining to each of the 
dimensions.
The same procedure of FCM shall be obtained to find the associational 
impacts between one factor over another concerning the D1 dimension, and 
this facilitates focused decision‑making. In a similar fashion, the connec‑
tion matrices concerning other dimensions and the associational impacts 
concerning D2 and D3 shall be determined as in Table 1.1.
The general representation of the factors with fuzzy soft sets integration 
for making focused decisions is given with Ai factors and Ej dimensions as 
in Table 1.2.
1.3  FOCUSED DECISION‑MAKING ON 
INSTITUTIONAL INITIATIVES TOWARD 
HOLISTIC GROWTH OF THE STUDENTS 
IN THE POST‑PANDEMIC PERIOD
In this section, the proposed integrated decision‑making model is validated 
with a decision‑making problem on institutional initiatives toward the 
holistic growth of students in the post‑pandemic period. Generally, every 
institution is striving hard to foster the holistic growth of their students 
by triggering several new initiatives. If holistic growth is the main focus, 
then the institutions must be well aware of the five dimensions of holistic

Integrated fuzzy soft FCM approach in focused decision‑making  7
growth namely, Physical Emotional, Social, Spiritual and Mental. The ini‑
tiatives that are taken by the institution must cater to all these dimensions 
of the students to channel the path for promoting holistic growth. All the 
initiatives that are proposed by an institution shall not be put into practice 
due to internal and external constraints, also the conditions of feasibility 
add on to it. Henceforth, the initiatives are to be ranked concerning their 
inter‑associational impacts on the dimensions too, and this has routed out 
focused decision‑making. The institutional initiatives are considered as the 
factors of the focused decision‑making problem.
A1 – Creating exposure to students on the recent academic endeavors 
institutionalized by higher educational institutions
A2 – Building ambiance in for dents to accommodate the changes in 
educational technology
A3  –  Conducting programs catering to the skill development of the 
students
A4 – Reinforcing the students with moral values and ethical principles
A5 – Facilitating a conducive environment for the personality develop‑
ment of the student community
A6 – Arranging counseling and mentoring sessions to overcome the stu‑
dent’s hitches
A7 – Lessening academic burdens with much focus on practical training 
and experience
A8 – Initiating interactive classes with various academic experts through 
networking
A9 – Framing of curriculum meeting the present demands of the students
A10 – Involving students in various cultural and other relishing activities 
to refresh and revive them
The comprehensive connection matrix comprising the associational impacts 
concerning the five dimensions of holistic growth is given in Table 1.3.
The above connection matrix shall be expressed like fuzzy soft sets rep‑
resentation. In this case, the dimensions are considered as the attributes 
{E1, E2, E3, E4, E5}, and the universal set comprises the factors {A1, A2, 
…A10}.
The fuzzy soft representations of the connection matrix between the 
­factors concerning the dimensions are as in Table 1.4.
The connection matrices concerning various dimensions of holistic 
growth of the student community can be derived from the above compre‑
hensive matrix. The connection matrix concerns the Physical dimension of 
the holistic growth of the student community.
Connection matrix of the Physical dimension of the holistic growth of the 
student community is given in Table 1.5.

1.4  DISCUSSIONS
The ranking of the factors with (E1, E2, E3, E4, E5) and without (G) dimen‑
sional discrimination is presented in Figure 1.2. Here, the notation of G 
represents the considerations of the dimensions without discrimination, it 
means the total effects of the factors over one another are considered. The 
ranking results of the factors with and without dimensional discrimination 
presented graphically shall be interpreted in the following manner. Let us 
consider factor A1, it occupies Rank 7 in the case of without dimensional 
discrimination, and in the case of dimensional discrimination, it occupies 
Ranks 4, 3, 2, 10 and 6 when the physical, emotional, social, spiritual and 
mental dimensions of the holistic growth of student community are consid‑
ered, respectively. In this way, the ranking of other factors shall be analyzed.

1.5  CONCLUSION
This research work introduces a new form of representation in FCM. The 
proposed integrated fuzzy soft FCM is applied in focused decision‑making, 
and this has paved the way for making decisions in considering various fac‑
ets of decision‑making concept. This model shall be extended by incorpo‑
rating a hyper soft set approach which shall accommodate more attributes 
and sub‑attributes.

2.1  INTRODUCTION
Decision‑making is one of the deliberate activities that takes place at all 
phases of production processes. Supplier selection is one of the crucial 
steps of the pre‑production phases as it plays a significant role in enhancing 
the quality of production. As the attribute of quality is an expected vital 
component in the perspective of the consumers, the decision‑makers must 
make an optimal selection of the suppliers to produce quality products. 
Researchers have applied several multi criteria decision making (MCDM) 
methods to supplier selection problems. A decision‑making problem in sup‑
plier selection is characterized by alternatives, criteria and suitable method 
of ranking the alternatives. In every decision‑making environment, there 
are two ways of assuming the criterion weights: one is equal criterion 
weights and the other is unequal criterion weights. If the significance of all 
the criteria is considered equal, then the criterion weights are assumed to 
be equal. If the criteria are graded based on their significance, then the cri‑
terion weights are assumed to be unequal. The objectives of every MCDM 
method are to find the criterion weights and to rank the alternatives. Some 
of the MCDM methods are exclusively applied to find the criterion weights, 
especially AHP, CRITIC and a few other methods of this kind also exist in 
the literature. Some of the other MCDM methods developed by researchers 
to rank the alternatives are TOPSIS, VIKOR, MARICA and many others.
In general, a decision‑making environment cannot be defined under deter‑
ministic conditions at all times as the criteria satisfaction rate of the alterna‑
tives cannot be specified by discrete values at all instances. This is the point 
where the concept of uncertainty creeps as an obstacle in formulating the 
initial decision‑making matrix. The problem of uncertainty is resolved by 
the notion of fuzzy sets introduced by Zadeh [1]. Fuzzy integrated MCDM 
methods are more viable and compatible in comparison to deterministic 
MCDM methods under a crisp environment, as the former leavens the 
complexity in handling uncertain data. Supplier selection problems are 
also discussed under a fuzzy environment, and the method of Fuzzy AHP 
(FAHP) is used predominantly to determine the criterion weights along 

18  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
with the common ranking methods such as TOPSIS, VIKOR, MOORA, 
DEMATEL and many other. On analyzing the research works on supplier 
selection, the application of new methods under a fuzzy environment is not 
explored except with very few repeated combinations of FAHP with other 
ranking methods. This has motivated the authors to develop a combination 
of FAHP and fuzzy SPOTIS ranking methods to solve the decision‑making 
problem in supplier selection.
In this research work, an integrated MCDM method is developed with 
the introduction of the fuzzy SPOTIS method and the application of the 
proposed method to the decision‑making problem on the supplier selection 
of cattle feed is presented with real data sets. The remaining content is pre‑
sented as follows: Section 2.2 comprises the literature review of the applica‑
tions of FAHP integrated with other MCDM methods in supplier selection, 
Section 2.3 sketches the methodology of the proposed method, Section 2.4 
applies the proposed method to the decision‑making problems on supplier 
selection of cattle feed, Section 2.5 discusses the results of the findings and 
the last section concludes the chapter with future scope of the research work
2.2  LITERATURE REVIEW
This section presents the applications of Fuzzy AHP integrated with other 
MCDM methods of ranking in supplier selection. As the contributions of FAHP 
are many in number, the literature review is limited to very recent research 
works. The method of fuzzy AHP is commonly applied in supplier selection 
problems subjected to various domains and Table 2.1 presents the same.
The method of FAHP is also integrated with other ranking MCDM 
methods to rank the suppliers. The contributions of the researchers in this 
aspect are shown in Table 2.2.
Table 2.1  Applications of FAHP in Supplier Selection

From Table 2.2, it is clear that the method of FAHP is widely applied 
in almost all fields of decision‑making. To the best of our knowledge, the 
method of FAHP is not integrated with the method of SPOTIS.
The MCDM method of SPOTIS introduced by Dezert et al. [34] is one of the 
MCDM methods with less complexity and it best fits into the MCDM charac‑
terization. This method is free from rank reversal, and it defines the bounds of 
the score values of the criteria to facilitate the comparison of the alternatives 
with the ideal solution. The MCDM problems without the bounds of the crite‑
rion score values are termed as ill‑defined MCDM, and this method of SPOTIS 
helps in finding optimal solutions to such types of problems. The method of 
SPOTIS is applied to make decisions on any kind of decision‑­making prob‑
lem, but this method is discussed only under a crisp sense i.e., if the criterion 
values are deterministic in nature, but what happens if the criterion values are

represented as linguistic variables with fuzzy quantification. In this case, the

this research work. Also, the method of FAHP is not integrated with SPOTIS, 
and henceforth in this chapter, an integrated FAHP & FSPOTIS is developed 
to make optimal decisions on supplier selection problem.

2.4  APPLICATION OF THE PROPOSED INTEGRATED 
METHOD IN SUPPLIER SELECTION
In this section, the proposed integrated FAHP & FSPOTIS is applied to the 
decision‑making problem on supplier selection of cattle feed.
2.4.1  Problem definition
Cattle feed is one of the essential factors of livestock productivity. The ade‑
quate supply and the quality of cattle feed is very much needed for increasing 
the production of milk and milk products. In general, every farm manager 
will consider the criteria of cost and quality as important in choosing the 
suppliers. But on intense analysis, the aspects of reliability, delivery service,

The above procedure shall be applied with different α sets for each cri‑
terion. This attempt is made to rank the alternatives with different bound 
values of the criteria based on the choice of the ‘α’ values. The obtained 
bound values based on the choice of the ‘α’ values indicate the expected per‑
formance rate of each criterion from the perspective of the decision‑makers. 
The ranking results obtained by considering criterion bound values with 
the same values of α differ from that of the results obtained by considering 
bound values with different α sets. The occurrences of such differences in 
the ranking results are quite natural, and it is subjected to the expectations 
of the decision‑maker.
2.6  CONCLUSION
This chapter proposes the FSPOTIS which is an extended fuzzy version of 
the SPOTIS MCDM. This newly developed method integrated with FAHP 
is applied in ranking the suppliers of cattle feed. The method of FSPOTIS is 
discussed under several considerations of α values. This integrated method 
is robust in nature and also it is realistic as it deals with a linguistic deci‑
sion matrix. This research work has good scope as the proposed method 
of FSPOTIS shall be integrated with other MCDM methods of criterion 
weight computation. The efficacy of the proposed method shall be tested 
using other MCDM methods.

Chapter 3
Parametric decision‑making 
in a fuzzy environment
Michael Gr. Voskoglou
3.1  INTRODUCTION: THE 
DECISION‑MAKING PROCESS
Problems appear frequently in everyday life having more than one feasible 
solution, i.e. solutions satisfying all the constraints related to the problem’s 
data, as well as all the natural constraints imposed by the environment. For 
example, if x denotes the quantity of a good and the problem states that the 
stock of this good must not exceed 50 units, a feasible solution must satisfy 
the inequality 0 ≤ x ≤50.
Decision‑making (DM) is the process of obtaining, with the help of a 
suitable criterion, the problem’s optimal solution, i.e. the best among its 
feasible solutions. The choice of the corresponding criterion is usually not 
unique, depending upon the decision‑maker’s desired goals, e.g. an optimis‑
tic or a conservative criterion, etc.
DM is a fundamental process in a great variety of human activities. The 
rapid technological progress, the sudden changes in the status of the local 
and international economic environment, the globalization of modern soci‑
ety and other related reasons led to a continuously increasing complexity of 
everyday life problems. As a result, the DM process becomes in many cases 
a very difficult task, so that it is impossible to be based on the decision‑mak‑
er’s experience, intuition and skills only, as it happened in earlier times. 
From the beginning of the 1950s, a progressive development started of a 
systematic methodology for the DM process which is based on Probability, 
Statistics, Economics, Psychology, etc., and is termed Statistical Decision 
Theory (SDT) (Liese & Miescke, 2008).
Many books have been written for helping decision‑makers to make 
smarter choices easier and quicker, e.g. (Barker, 2011, Heath & Heath, 
2013), etc. According to the nowadays existing standards, the DM process 
involves the following steps:

Parametric decision-making in a fuzzy environment  31
•	 d1: Analysis of the decision problem, i.e. understanding, simplify‑
ing and reformulating it in a way enabling the application of the DM 
process.
•	 d2: Collection and interpretation of all the necessary information 
related to the problem.
•	 d3: Determination of the problem’s feasible solutions.
•	 d4: Choice of the problem’s optimal solution (DM) with the help of the 
suitable, according to the decision‑maker’s goals, criterion.
•	 d5: Verification of the chosen decision on the basis of the results 
obtained by applying it in practice.
The last step d5, however, due to its depth and importance for administra‑
tive rationalism, is usually examined separately from the other steps of the 
DM process. Note that the first three steps of the DM process are con‑
tinuous in the sense that the completion of each one of them usually needs 
some time, during which the decision‑maker’s reasoning is characterized by 
transitions between hierarchically neighboring steps. Accordingly, the flow 
diagram of the DM process is represented in Figure 3.1.
In this chapter, extending a result of Maji et al. (2002), we develop a 
model of parametric DM using soft sets (SSs), grey numbers and neutro‑
sophic triplets as tools (Voskoglou, 2023). The rest of the chapter is orga‑
nized as follows: In Section 3.2, we study the process of DM under fuzzy 
conditions and we present an example solved with the help of the classical 
fuzzy DM criterion of Bellman & Zadeh (1970). Section 3.3 contains the 
mathematical background about neutrosophic sets, SSs and grey numbers, 
needed for the understanding of the rest of the chapter. Our parametric DM 
method is developed in Section 3.4 and the chapter closes with Section 3.5 
containing the final conclusions and hints for future research.
3.2  DECISION‑MAKING UNDER FUZZY CONDITIONS
Zadeh (1965) introduced the concept of fuzzy set (FS) for the purpose of 
tackling the existing in everyday life partial truths (e.g. almost true, rather 
pretty) and of expressing mathematical definitions having no clear bound‑
aries (e.g. high mountains, clever people)
Figure 3.1  Flow diagram of the DM process. (Voskoglou, 2020.)

32  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
Definition 3.1
Αn FS A on the universe U is defined as a set of the ordered pairs of 
the form
	
A = {(x, m(x)), x ∈ U}	
(3.1)
In Eq. (3.1) m: U → [0, 1] is the membership function of A and m(x) 
is called the membership degree of x in A, for all elements x of U. 
The closer m(x) to 1, the better x satisfies the characteristic property 
of A. For reasons of simplicity, many authors identify an FS with its 
membership function.
The membership function of an FS is obviously a generalization 
of the characteristic function of crisp sets. A disadvantage of FSs, 
however, is the existing difficulty in defining properly their member‑
ship functions. In fact, there is not any specific rule about this and 
the methods used are usually statistical or empirical, highly based 
on each observer’s personal criteria. For defining the FS of the high 
mountains, for example, one may consider as high all the moun‑
tains with heights greater than 2,500 m, whereas another one may 
consider all those with heights greater than 3,000 m. Consequently, 
the first observer will assign membership degree 1 to all mountains 
higher than 2,500 m, whereas the second one will assign member‑
ship degrees <1 to all mountains having heights between 2,500 and 
3,000 m. Analogous differences with respect to the two observers 
are expected to occur between the membership degrees of the other 
mountains as well. The only restriction for the definition of the mem‑
bership degree of an FS is to be compatible with the common logic; 
otherwise, the FS would not give a creditable description of the cor‑
responding real situation. This could happen, for example, in the 
previous case if one assigned membership degrees >0.5 to mountains 
with heights <500 m.
Most of the notions and operations on crisp sets are extended in a 
natural way to FSs.
Based on the concept of FS, Zadeh (1973) also introduced the infi‑
nite‑valued fuzzy logic (FL), in which the truth values are modelled by 
numbers in the unit interval [0, 1]. FL completes and extends the tra‑
ditional Aristotle’s (384–322 BC) bivalent logic (Kosko, 1993), which 
used to be for many centuries the basic tool of human reasoning, lead‑
ing to the growth of science and human civilization all this time
Uncertainty is defined as the shortage of precise knowledge or com‑
plete information on the data describing the state of a current situation. 

Parametric decision-making in a fuzzy environment  33
It was only in the second stage that FS theory and FL were used to 
embrace uncertainty modelling. This happened when membership 
functions were reinterpreted as possibility distributions (Zadeh, 1978, 
Dubois & Prade, 2001). Possibility theory is an uncertainty theory 
for handling incomplete information (Dubois & Prade, 2006). Zadeh 
(1978) clarified the relationship between possibility and probability, 
noticing that what is probable must preliminarily be possible.
Probability theory used for many years the unique tool in hands 
of the experts for dealing with problems connected to uncertainty. 
Probability, however, was proved to be sufficient only for tackling 
the cases of uncertainty that are due to randomness (Kosko, 1990). 
Randomness characterizes events with known outcomes which, how‑
ever, cannot be predicted in advance, e.g. the games of chance. FSs, 
apart from randomness, tackle also successfully the uncertainty due to 
vagueness, which is created when one is unable to distinguish between 
two properties, such as “a good student” and “a mediocre student”. 
For general facts on FSs, FL and the uncertainty connected to them, 
we refer to Klir & Folger (1988).
The DM problems are obviously connected with a degree of uncer‑
tainty. Moreover, in a variety of real‑life situations, DM takes place 
under fuzzy conditions because either the goals of the correspond‑
ing problems or/and the existing constraints are not clearly defined. 
Several methods and techniques for successful DM in the fuzzy envi‑
ronment have been reported in the literature, e.g. Bellman & Zadeh 
(1970), Alcantud (2018), Zhu & Ren (2022), etc. A typical form of a 
fuzzy DM problem involves its fuzzy goal G and a finite number of 
fuzzy constraints Ci, i = 1, 2, …, n. Such a problem is presented and 
solved in the following example:
Example 3.1
A company wants to employ the best among four candidates A, B, C 
and D (fuzzy goal G) under the fuzzy constraints that his salary will 
not be very high (C1) and that his residence will be in close driving dis‑
tance from the company’s offices (C2). The annual salary demands of the 
four candidates are 39, 35, 24 and 18 thousand euros, respectively. The 
company’s analysts, taking into account the candidates’ qualifications, 
expressed goal G and the constraints C1, C2 as FSs in the following form:
	
G = {(A, 0.9), (B, 0.8), (C, 0.6), (D, 0.5)},  
	
C1 = {(A, 0.1), (B, 0.3), (C, 0.6), (D, 1)}
	
C2 = {(A, 0.4), (B, 0.5), (C, 0.9), (D, 0.7)}.	
(3.2)

34  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
Which is the best choice for the company?
Solution: The universal set of the discourse here is the set U = {A, B, C, D}.  
According to the classical criterion of Bellman & Zadeh (1970) for DM 
in the fuzzy environment, the best company’s choice corresponds to the 
element of U with the greatest membership degree in the fuzzy decision 
F = G ∩ C1 ∩ C2 (intersection of FSs).
Let us denote by mG,

C2 and F of the FSs, respectively. It is well known then (Klir & Folger, 
1988, Section 2.4) that

F = {(A, 0.1), (B, 0.3), (C, 0.6), (D, 0.5)}

3.3  NEUTROSOPHIC SETS, SSS AND GREY NUMBERS
Following Zadeh’s FSs, several theories have been developed for the pur‑
pose of tackling more effectively all the forms of the existing uncertainty. 
The main among those theories are briefly reviewed in Voskoglou (2019). 
None of these theories, however, is suitable for tackling alone all the forms 
of the existing uncertainty, but the combination of all of them provides a 
sufficient framework for this purpose. Here, we present chosen elements 
from these theories which are needed for the understanding of the rest of 
the present chapter.
3.3.1  Neutrosophic sets
Atanassov (1986) added to Zadeh’s membership degree the degree of 
non‑membership and extended FS to the notion of intuitionistic FS (IFS). 
Smarandache (1998), inspired by the appearance in real‑life neutrali‑
ties – like <young, mature, old>, <small, medium, large>, etc. – extended in 
1995, the IFS to the concept of neutrosophic set (NS) by introducing the 
degree of indeterminacy or neutrality. The word “neutrosophy” is a syn‑
thesis of the words “neutral´ and the Greek “sophia” (wisdom) and means 
“the knowledge of neutral thought”. The simplest form of an NS is defined 
as follows:
Definition 3.2
A single‑valued NS (SVNS) A in the universe U is of the form
A = {(x, T(x), I(x), F(x)): x ∈ U, T(x), I(x), F(x) ∈ [0,1], 
0 ≤ T(x) + I(x) + F(x) ≤ 3}	
(3.9)
In Eq. (3.9), T(x), I(x) and F(x) are the degrees of truth (or member‑
ship), indeterminacy (or neutrality) and falsity (or non‑membership) 

36  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
of x in A, respectively, called the neutrosophic components of x. For 
simplicity, we write A < T, I, F>.
Indeterminacy is defined to be in general everything that exists 
between the opposites of truth and falsity (Smarandache, 2021).
Example 3.2
Let U be the set of the students of a class and let A be the SVNS of the 
good students of the class, then each student x is characterized by a 
neutrosophic triplet (t, i, f) with respect to A, with t, i, f in [0, 1]. For 
example, x(0.8, 0.1, 0.3) ∈ A means that there is a 70% belief that x is 
a good student, but at the same time, there exists a 10% doubt about 
it and a 30% belief that x is not a good student. In particular, x (0, 1, 
0) ∈ A means that we know absolutely nothing about the quality of 
student x (new student).
If the sum T(x) + I(x) + F(x) < 1, then it leaves room for incomplete 
information about x, if it is equal to 1 for complete information and if 
it is >1 for inconsistent (i.e. contradiction tolerant) information about 
x. An SVNS may contain simultaneously elements leaving room to all 
the previous types of information. All notions and operations defined 
on FSs are naturally extended to SVNSs (Wang et al., 2010).
The summation of neutrosophic triplets is equivalent to the union 
of NSs. That is why the neutrosophic summation and implicitly its 
extension to neutrosophic scalar multiplication can be defined in many 
ways, equivalently to the known literature neutrosophic union opera‑
tors (Smarandache, 2016). For the needs of the present work, writing 
the elements of an SVNS A in the form of neutrosophic triplets and 
considering them simply as ordered triplets, we define addition and 
scalar product as follows:
Definition 3.3
Let (t1, i1, f1), (t2, i2, f2) be in A and let k be a positive number. Then:
	
The sum (t1, i1, f1) + (t2, i2, f2) = (t1 + t2, i1 + i2, f1 + f2)		
(3.10)
	
The scalar product k(t1, i1, f1) = (kt1, k i1, kf1)	
	
(3.11)
Remark 3.2
The summation and scalar product of the elements of an SVNS A with 
respect to Definition 3.3 need not be a closed operation in A, since it 
may happen that (t1 + t2) + (i1 + i2) + (f1 + f2) > 3 or kt1 + k i1 + kf1 > 3. With 
the help of Definition 3.3, however, one can define in A the mean 
value of a finite number of elements of A as follows:

Parametric decision-making in a fuzzy environment  37
Definition 3.4
Let A be an SVNS and let (t1, i1, f1), (t2, i2, f2), …, (tk, ik, fk) be a finite 
number of elements of A. Assume that (ti, ii, fi) appears ni times in an 
application, i = 1, 2, …, k. Set n = n1 + n2 +… + nk. Then, the mean value 
of all these elements of A is defined to be

3.3.2  Soft sets
The existing difficulty of defining properly the membership function of an 
FS also holds for all the extensions of FSs involving membership degrees, 
like IFSs and NSs. To tackle this problem, the concept of interval‑valued FS 
(IVFS) was introduced in 1975. An IVFS is defined by mapping the universe 
U to the set of the closed subintervals of [0, 1] (Dubois & Prade, 2005). In 
rough set theory (Pawlak, 1991), the previous problem is overpassed by 
using instead of membership functions a pair of crisp sets giving the lower 
and the upper bounds of the original set, etc.
Molodtsov (1999) introduced the concept of SS for tackling uncertainty 
in a parametric manner, which, therefore, does not need too the use of a 
membership function. An SS is defined as follows:
Definition 3.5
Let E be a set of parameters, let A be a subset of E, and let f be a map 
from A into the power set P(U) of the universe U. Then, the SS (f, A) 
in U is defined as the set of the ordered pairs
	
(f, A) = {(e, f(e)): e ∈ A}	
	
	
	
	
(3.13)
The name “soft” is due to the fact that the form of (f, A) depends on the 
parameters of A. For each e ∈ A, its image f(e) in P(U) is called the value 
set of e in (f, A), while f is called the approximation function of (f, A).
Example 3.3
Let U = {P1, P2, P3} be a set of football players and let E = {e1, e2, e3} be the 
set of parameters e1 = good, e2 = fast and e3 = experienced. Let us further 
assume that P1, P2 are good, P3 is experienced and P2 and P3 are fast play‑
ers. Then, a map f: E → P(U) is defined by f(e1)  = {P1, P2}, f(e2) = {P2, P3} 
and f(e3) = {P3}. Therefore, the SS (f, E) in U is the set of the ordered pairs.
	
(f, E) = {(e1, {P1, P2}), (e2, {P2, P3}, (e3, {P3}}	
(3.14)

38  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
Maji et al. (2003) introduced a tabular representation of SSs in the 
form of a binary matrix in order to be stored easily in a computer’s 
memory. For example, the tabular representation of the SS (f, E) of the 
previous example is given in Table 3.1.
An FS in U with membership function y = m(x) is an SS in U of the 
form (f, [0, 1]), where f(α) = {x ∈ U: m(x) ≥ α} is the corresponding a‑cut 
of the FS, for each α in [0, 1]. Consequently, the concept of SS is a gen‑
eralization of the concept of FS. All notions and operations defined on 
FSs are extended in a natural way to SSs (Maji et al., 2003).
3.3.3  Grey numbers
The theory of grey systems (Deng, 1982) introduces an alternative way of 
managing the uncertainty in case of approximate data. A grey system is 
understood to be any system that lacks information, such as a structure 
message, operation mechanism or/and behavior document.
Closed real intervals are used for performing the necessary calculations 
in grey systems. In fact, a closed real interval [x, y] could be considered as 
representing a real number T, termed as a grey number (GN), whose exact 
value in [x, y] is unknown. We write then T ∈ [x, y]. A GN T, however, is 
frequently accompanied by a whitenization function f: [x, y] → [0, 1], such 
that, if f(a) approaches 1, then a in [x, y] approaches the unknown value of 
T. If no whitenization function is defined, it is logical to consider as a repre‑
sentative crisp approximation of the GN A the real number
	
( ) =

known arithmetic of the real intervals (Moore et al., 1995). In this work, 
we are going to make use only of the addition of GNs and of the scalar mul‑
tiplication of a GN with a positive number, which are defined as follows:
Definition 3.6
Let A ∈ [x1, y1], B ∈ [x2, y2] be two GNs and let k be a positive num‑
ber. Then:

3.4  PARAMETRIC DECISION‑MAKING
Maji et al. (2002) introduced a method of parametric DM by using SSs as 
tools and representing their tabular form as a binary matrix. When some 
or all of the parameters used for the characterization of the elements of the 
set of the discourse are of fuzzy texture (beautiful and cheap houses in their 
example), their method does not always give the best solution to the cor‑
responding DM problem. This happens because they replace the parameters 
in the tabular form of the corresponding SS with the binary elements 0, 1. 
In other words, their method, although it starts with a fuzzy framework 
(SSs), continues by using bivalent logic for obtaining the required decision, 
e.g. beautiful or not beautiful! In order to tackle this problem, we modified 
the method of Maji et al. by using either triangular fuzzy numbers (TFNs) 
(Voskoglou, 2022a) or GNs (Voskoglou, 2022b, Section 5.3) instead of the 
binary elements in the tabular form of the corresponding SS.
In everyday life, however, cases appear in which the decision‑maker 
has doubts about the correctness of the qualitative parameters assigned to 
some or all of the elements of the set of the discourse (e.g. very beautiful, 
rather beautiful, etc.). In order to study more efficiently the DM process in 
such cases, we introduced NSs and we replaced the binary elements of the 
method of Maji et al. by neutrosophic triplets (Voskoglou, 2023). Here, we 
present a parametric DM problem illustrating all the previous steps
3.4.1  The method of Maji et al.
Let us consider the following example:
Example 3.4
A company wants to choose a sales manager among six candidates, say 
S1, S2, S3, S4, S5 and S6. The desired qualifications of the sales manager 
are to be well‑educated, younger than 40 years, having a driving license 
and experience. Assume that S1, S2 and S6 are the well‑educated candi‑
dates, S2, S3, S5 and S6 are the candidates being younger than 40 years, 
S3 and S5 are the candidates having a driving license and S4 is the unique 
experienced candidate. Which is the best choice for the company?
Solution: Let U be the set of the six candidates. Consider the param‑
eters e1 = well educated, e2 = younger than 40 years, e3 = with a driving 
license and e4 = experienced and set E = {e1, e2, e3, e4}. Define the map f: 
E → P(U) by f(e1) = {S1, S2, S6}, f(e2) = {S2, S3, S5, S6}, f(e3) = {S3, S5} and 
f(e4) = {S4}. Then, the tabular form of the SS (f, E) is shown in Table 3.2.

40  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
The choice value of each candidate is calculated by adding the binary 
elements of the row of Table 9.2 to which he belongs. Candidates S1 
and S4, therefore, have choice value 1 and all the others have choice 
value 2. Consequently, the right decision is to choose one of the candi‑
dates S2, S3, S5 or S6.
3.4.2  The decision‑making method using GNs
The previous decision, obtained by the method of Maji et al., is obviously 
not very helpful for the company. Observe, however, that the parameters e1 
and e4, in contrast to e2 and e3, have not a bivalent texture. A candidate, 
for example, could be rather well‑educated, or rather experienced, etc. This 
inspired us to characterize the candidates’ qualifications with respect to 
the parameters e1 and e4 in the tabular matrix of the SS (f, E) by using the 
linguistic grades A = excellent, B = very good, C = good, D = mediocre and 
F = not satisfactory instead of the binary elements 0, 1. To show how one 
works in this case for making the right decision, let us modify Example 3.4 
as follows:
Example 3.5
Reconsider Example 3.4 and assume that the analysts of the company, 
after a more careful inspection of the candidates’ qualifications, decided 
to use Table 3.3 instead of Table 3.2 for the DM process. Which is the 
best choice for the company in this case?
Solution: Assign to each of the qualitative grades A, B, C, D, F a 
GN, denoted for simplicity by the same letter, as follows: A = [0.85, 
1], B = [0.75, 0.84], C = [0.6, 0.74], D = [0.5, 0.59], F = [0, 0.49]. Then, 
from Table 3.3, one calculates, with the help of formulas (3.15), (3.16) 
and (3.17), the choice value of each candidate in the following way: S1: 
1 + V(C) = 1+

 = 1.67, S2: 2 + V(F) = 2.245, S3: 2 + V(2C) = 3.34, 
S4: 1 + V(D)= 1.545, S5: 2 + V(D + C) = 3.215, S6: 2 + V(D)=2.545. The 
right decision, therefore, is to choose the candidate S3.

Remark 3.3
The choice of the qualitative grades A, B, C, D, F, as well as of the 
intervals for translating them in the numerical scale 0 − 1, correspond 
to generally accepted standards. The decision‑maker, however, could 
use, with respect to his/her goals, more or less qualitative grades (e.g. 
by adding E between D and F, etc.) and could also change the cor‑
responding intervals (e.g. by setting A = [0.9, 1], B = [0.8, 0.89], C= 
[0.7, 0.79], D = [0.6, 0.69], F = [0, 0.59], or otherwise). Such changes, 
however, do not affect the generality of our method.
3.4.3  Neutrosophic decision‑making
When the decision‑maker has doubts about the correctness of the qualita‑
tive parameters assigned to some or all of the elements of the set of the dis‑
course, the best way to perform the DM process is to use NSs. In Example 
3.5, for instance, the decision‑maker could define in the universe U the 
NSs of the well‑educated and of the experienced candidates by assigning 
suitable neutrosophic triplets to each candidate. In order to have complete 
information, one must choose to have t + i + f = 1, for each triplet (t, i, f). 
Then, the decision‑maker could continue the DM process by replacing in 
Table 3.3 the qualitative grades by the corresponding neutrosophic triplets 
and the binary elements 0, 1 by the neutrosophic triplets (0, 0, 1) and (1, 0, 
0), respectively. This process is illustrated by Example 3.6.
Example 3.6
Reconsider Example 3.5 and assume that the analysts of the company, 
being not sure about the qualitative grades assigned to each of the six 
candidates, decided to proceed by replacing them by neutrosophic trip‑
lets. The tabular matrix of the DM process in these cases takes the 
form of Table 3.4. Which is the best decision for the company now?

Solution: The choice value of each candidate in this case is the mean 
value of the neutrosophic triplets of the line of Table 9.4 in which he 
belongs. Thus, by Eq. (3.12), the choice value of S1 is equal to 1
4

the same way, one finds that the choice values of S2, S3, S4, S5 and S6 
are (0.55, 0.005, 0.4), (0.775, 0.15, 0.075), (0.375, 0.05, 0.575), (0.775, 
0.1, 0.125) and (0.6, 0.1, 0.3) respectively.
In this case, the company’s analysts could use either an optimistic 
criterion by choosing the candidate with the greatest truth degree or 
a conservative criterion by choosing the player with the lower falsity 
degree. Consequently, using the optimistic criterion, they must choose 
one of the candidates S3 and S5, whereas using the conservative crite‑
rion, they must choose Candidate S3. The combination of the two cri‑
teria, therefore, leads to the choice of candidate S3. Observe, however, 
that, since the indeterminacy degree of S3 is 0.15 and of S5 is 0.1, there 
is a slightly greater doubt about the qualifications of S3 with respect to 
the qualifications of S5. In other words, the choice of S3 is connected 
with a slightly greater risk.
3.5  FINAL CONCLUSIONS AND HINTS 
FOR FUTURE RESEARCH
The goals and/or the existing constraints in several DM problems of every‑
day life are not clearly defined. As a result, the DM process in such cases 
takes place in the fuzzy environment. Several methods and techniques have 
been reported in the literature for DM under fuzzy conditions, and the pres‑
ent chapter is devoted to the solution of DM problems of such kind. The 
discussion performed here leads to the following conclusions:
•	 A serious disadvantage of FSs is that the definition of their mem‑
bership function is not unique depending on the personal beliefs of 
each observer. This disadvantage is transferred to the DM process 
under fuzzy conditions when the goals and /or the constraints of the

corresponding problem are expressed in the form of FSs. A popular 
DM method in this case is the application of the criterion of Bellman 
and Zadeh, according to which the optimal fuzzy decision is the 
intersection of all the problem’s goals and constraints. This method, 
however, is not unique, since several other logical approaches can be 
applied, which could lead to different results.
•	 In order to overcome this difficulty, Maji et al. proposed a parametric 
DM method, which is based on the introduction of a set E of param‑
eters characterizing the elements of the set U of all possible solutions 
of the corresponding DM problem, the definition with respect to E of 
a suitable SS in U and the use of its tabular representation T as a tool 
for the DM process.
•	 When some or all of the parameters of E, however, are not of binary 
(yes or no) but of fuzzy texture, the method of Maji et al. may not lead 
to the best decision. For this reason, we modified this method by replac‑
ing the binary elements of T corresponding to the fuzzy parameters by 
suitable GNs and by adding the remaining in each row of T binary ele‑
ments to the representative real values of the GNs appearing in it
•	 Further, when some or all of the parameters of E are of fuzzy texture, 
but the decision‑maker has doubts about the qualitative characteriza‑
tions assigned to the elements of U with respect to these parameters, 
we replaced all the elements of T by suitable neutrosophic triplets and 
we obtained the optimal decision by calculating the mean value of the 
neutrosophic triplets of the rows of T.
The results obtained in this and earlier author’s works give a strong indi‑
cation that hybrid methods applied to several situations in fuzzy environ‑
ments could give better results, not only for DM, as it happened here, but 
also for assessment – e.g. see Voskoglou (2022b), Section 5.2, Voskoglou 
et al. (2022), etc. – and probably for many other topics. This is, therefore, a 
promising area for further research.

Chapter 4
Distance measures on 
trapezoidal intuitionistic 
fuzzy multi-numbers and 
application to multi-criteria 
decision-making problems
Irfan Deli and Hümeyra Karadöl
4.1  INTRODUCTION
Since the fuzzy sets [1] have a single membership value on [0, 1] for an 
element of the universe, it creates difficulties in the construction of some 
problems. Therefore, multi-fuzzy sets, which is a different generalization of 
fuzzy sets, were first developed on the multisets of Yager [2] by Miyamoto 
[3,4]. Then, Sebastian and Ramakrishnan [5] studied multi-fuzzy sub‑
groups and normal multi-fuzzy subgroups. The intuitionistic fuzzy multi‑
sets [7], as a generation of ordinary intuitionistic fuzzy sets [6] and fuzzy 
multisets [3,4], can express and process uncertainty much better. The intu‑
itionistic fuzzy multisets, characterized both by membership degrees and by 
non-membership degrees, is a more flexible way to cope with uncertainty. 
Therefore, Shinoj and John [7] generalized the concept of fuzzy multisets 
by using intuitionistic fuzzy sets, i.e., they proposed intuitionistic fuzzy 
multisets including basic operations such as union, intersection, addition, 
multiplication, etc. Moreover, Rajarajeswari and Uma [8,9] proposed 
some distance and similarity measures of intuitionistic fuzzy multisets and 
applied to medical diagnosis and pattern recognition problems. Das et al. 
[10] developed a different method for group multi-criteria decision-making 
under intuitionistic multi-fuzzy sets by constructing intuitionistic multi-
fuzzy matrices. Deepa [11] examined some new implication results related 
to intuitionistic fuzzy multisets. Fuzzy multisets and intuitionistic fuzzy 
multisets have gained much attention from past and later from researchers 
for applications in various fields as per Ref. [12–21].
Trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers [22], a fuzzy multiset on real numbers, 
have been studied in various fields in Ref. [22–25]. After the definition of 
trapezoidal fuzzy multi-number, Ulucay et al. Ref. [26] put forward trap‑
ezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy multi-numbers that are a special intuitionistic 
fuzzy multiset on real numbers that are more than one with the possibility 
of the same or the different membership and non-membership functions. 

Distance measures on trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers  47
As an extension of the trapezoidal fuzzy multi numbers, the trapezoidal 
intuitionistic fuzzy multi numbers is an effective tool for handling uncer‑
tainty and vagueness in decision-making problems. However, there are a 
few studies on trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy multi-numbers proposed by 
Ulucay et al. [26]. Therefore, in this study, we have developed a multi-­
criteria decision-making method under trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy 
multi-numbers. To do this, the notation of value and ambiguity of trap‑
ezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy multi-numbers based on (α, β)-cut sets are intro‑
duced. Then, some distance measures of trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy 
multi-numbers by using the concept of (α, β)-cut sets, value and ambiguity 
are developed. Finally, a method is proposed and a practical application of 
the proposed approach is also illustrated to demonstrate its effectiveness 
and applicability. This chapter is derived from the second author’s master’s 
­thesis [27] supervised by the second author.
4.2  BASIC TERMS AND OPERATIONS

related to fuzzy sets, fuzzy multisets and trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy 
multi-numbers.

(4.10)
4.3  DISTANCE MEASURES OF TIFM-NUMBERS
In the section, we examined the cut sets of TIFM-numbers including some 
desired properties. So, we proposed the concept of value and ambiguity of 
TIFM-numbers based on the cut sets. Some of it is quoted/adopted and/or 
inspired and/or generalized from [22–27].

4.4  AN APPROACH TO MULTIPLE CRITERIA 
DECISION-MAKING WITH TIFM-NUMBERS
In this section, we developed an algorithm to solve some multi-criteria-
making problems by using TIFM-numbers based on proposed distance 
measures. Some of it is quoted/adopted and/or inspired and/or generalized 
from Refs. [22–26].

Chapter 5
Trapezoidal fuzzy  
multi-aggregation operators based 
on Archimedean norms and their 
application to multi-attribute 
decision-making problems
Davut Kesen and Irfan Deli
5.1  INTRODUCTION
In 1965, the theory of fuzzy set was proposed by Zadeh [1] as a generaliza‑
tion of the classical set under ambiguous or uncertain information. In the 
proposed theory, elements can have a precise degree of membership or mem‑
bership functions that determine the membership degree of each element in 
a fuzzy set. Following the introduction of the theory, a few kinds of special 
fuzzy sets were proposed by many scientists on the real number set . For 
instance, Dijkman et al. [2] introduced some kind of fuzzy numbers contain‑
ing operations of fuzzy numbers. Dubois and Prade [3] reviewed works on 
fuzzy numbers proposed until then and they enlarged known operations 
on R to operations among fuzzy quantities. In the course of time, the sub‑
ject enlarged by scientists and some kind of fuzzy numbers were proposed 
and worked on. For example, Srinivasan et al. [4] submitted the median 
method as a new way to access the most optimal solution for a transporta‑
tion issue. Wang et al. [5] introduced the concept of discrete fuzzy num‑
bers with two dimensions that are based on the representation theorem. 
Yun et al. [6] introduced generalized triangular fuzzy numbers with some 
operations based on Zadeh’s extension principle. Dubois et al. [7] proposed 
new properties of probability-possibility transformation and proposed a jus‑
tification of symmetric triangular fuzzy numbers. Rezvani [8] calculated the 
ranking of exponential trapezoidal fuzzy numbers based on variance. Then, 
Roseline and Amirtharaj [9] proposed a new method for ranking general‑
ized trapezoidal fuzzy numbers, and they also proposed a generalized fuzzy 
Hungarian method to get the initial solution to a transportation problem. 
We can find more study examples given about trapezoidal fuzzy numbers in 
Refs. [10–15] and triangular fuzzy numbers in Refs. [16–25].
Because some realizations have more than one probability of the differ‑
ent or same membership functions, Ulucay et al. [26] developed trapezoidal 
fuzzy multi-numbers on real numbers set  which are an extension of fuzzy 
numbers and multi-fuzzy sets by letting the repeated occurrences of any 

94  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
element. Then, by defining the concept of value and ambiguity with α-cut 
sets of trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers, Keles [27] gave some similarity 
and distance measures on trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers and he intro‑
duced a multi-attribute decision-making problem under trapezoidal fuzzy 
multi-numbers based on proposed similarity and distance measures under 
cut sets. Sahin et al. [28] introduced a new multi-criteria decision-­making 
by defining dice vector similarity and weighted dice vector similarity mea‑
sure. Also, Sahin et al. [29] developed some similarity measures for trap‑
ezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers called weighted cosine similarity measure, 
hybrid vector similarity measure, Jaccard similarity measure, weighted 
Jaccard similarity measure, cosine similarity measure and weighted hybrid 
vector similarity measure. In 2020, Ulucay [30] defined new concepts of 
trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers such as similarity function and weighted 
similarity function. Then, Deli and Keles [31] described new distance mea‑
sures on trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers. In 2022, Kesen [32] introduced 
some arithmetic and geometric decision-making operators on trapezoidal 
fuzzy multi-numbers and applied these operators to multi-criteria decision-
making problems. Then, Kesen and Deli [33] introduced the weighted the 
Bonferroni harmonic mean operator on trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers 
and they applied the operator to a multi-criteria decision-making problem. 
In 2023, Sahin et al. [34] proposed a method by the centroid point of trap‑
ezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers for multi-criteria decision-making problems. 
Further, Deli and Kesen [35] introduced Bonferroni geometric mean opera‑
tor of trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers and they applied the operator to a 
multi-criteria decision-making problem.
In the course of the aggregation process, the selection of suitable opera‑
tional laws have a crucial role. The Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm supply 
a general rule of operational laws and more alternatives for decision-making. 
Until today, many kinds of t-conorm and t-norm have been derived from the 
Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm. For instance, Klir and Yuan [36] and 
Nguyen and Walker [37] proposed t-conorm and t-norm of the Algebraic, 
Einstein, Hamacher, Frank and so on. Based on Archimedean t-conorm and 
t-norm, Xia et al. [38] introduced Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm based 
intuitionistic fuzzy weighted arithmetic and weighted geometric operators. 
Zhang and Wu [39] introduced some Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm 
based interval-valued hesitant fuzzy weighted arithmetic and weighted geo‑
metric aggregation operators. Yu [40] came up with dual hesitant fuzzy 
weighted arithmetic and weighted geometric aggregation operators based on 
Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm operations in a dual hesitant fuzzy envi‑
ronment. Latter, Sarkar and Biswas [41] submitted Archimedean t-conorm 
and t-norm operations on Pythagorean hesitant fuzzy sets and gave a defini‑
tion of a collection of Archimedean t-conorm and t-norm based Pythagorean 
hesitant fuzzy weighted arithmetic and weighted geometric operators. In addi‑
tion to this, Sarkar and Biswas [42] gave an application of Archimedean 

Trapezoidal fuzzy multi-aggregation operators  95
t-conorm and t-norm on the interval-valued dual hesitant fuzzy information 
and introduced a class of aggregation operators.
5.1.1  Novelty
As we know, no studies about Archimedean norms on trapezoidal fuzzy 
numbers have been conducted until now. Therefore, we proposed trapezoi‑
dal multi-fuzzy aggregation operators based on Archimedean t-conorm and 
t-norm. Then, we applied them to MADM-problems.
5.1.2  Motivation and contribution
	
1.	The given operators based on Archimedean norms are introduced to 
propose a new decision-making method for solving MADM-problems 
with trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers.
	
2.	The main aim is to develop ATS-TFMWA operator and ATS-TFMWG 
operator for MADM-problems with trapezoidal fuzzy multi-numbers.
	
3.	A method is constructed under MADM-problems with trapezoidal 
fuzzy multi-numbers.
	
4.	An example of an application is given to show the effectiveness and 
advantage of the introduced method.
5.1.3  Organization of paper
↓ In Section 5.2, we gave an introduction of some basic definitions and 
propositions.
↓ In Section 5.3, we proposed ATS-TFMWA and ATS-TFMWG opera‑
tors and also, we discussed their desirable properties.
↓ In Section 5.4, we developed a method to solve multi-criteria decision-
making problems and then gave an example.
↓ In Section 5.5, we submitted a comparison table including the results 
of some existing methods.
 In Section 5.6, we proposed the conclusion.

Definition 5.3
[36] If function τ : [0, 1] × [0,1] → [0, 1] satisfies the following conditions, 
it is named a t-norm:
	
1.	τ(1, x) = x, for all x.
	
2.	τ(x, y) = τ (y, x), for all x and y.
	
3.	τ(x, τ(y, z))= τ (τ (x, y), z), for all x, y and z.
	
4.	If x ≤ x′ and y ≤ y′ then τ(x, y) ≤ τ (x′, y′).
Definition 5.4
[36] If function s: [0, 1] × [0, 1] → [0, 1] satisfies the following conditions, it 
is named a t-conorm:
	
1.	S(0, x) = x, for all x.
	
2.	S(x, y) = s(y, x), for all x and y.
	
3.	S(x, s(y, z)) = s(s(x, y), z), for all x, y and z.
	
4.	If x ≤ x′ and y ≤ y′ then s(x, y) ≤ s(x′, y′).
Definition 5.5
[26] Let ηT ∈ [0,1] and ε1, ε2, ε3, ε4 ∈ R such that ε1 ≤ ε2 ≤ ε3 ≤ ε4. Then, a 
generalized trapezoidal fuzzy number (GTF-number) T = ⟨(ε1, ε2, ε3, ε4); ηT⟩ 
is a special fuzzy set on the real number set R. Its membership functions 
are defined as;
	
µ

By considering the value of TFM-numbers, we can compare two TFM-
numbers T1 and T2 as follows:
If Val(T1) < Val(T2) then T1 <T2.
If Val(T1) > Val(T2) then T1 >T2.
If Val(T1) = Val(T2) then T1 =T2.
Definition 5.12
[36,49] If a t-norm function τ(x, y) is continuous and τ(x, x) < x for all x ∈ 
(0, 1), it is called Archimedean t-norm. If an Archimedean t-norm is strictly 
increasing in each variable for x, y ∈ (0, 1), it is called strict Archimedean 
t-norm. If a t-conorm function s(x, y) is continuous and s(x, x) > x for all 
x ∈ (0,1), it is called Archimedean t-conorm. If an Archimedean t-conorm 
is strictly increasing in each variable for x, y ∈ (0, 1), it is called strict 
Archimedean t-conorm.
In Ref. [50], a strict Archimedean t-norm can be rewritten by its additive 
generator h as

By inspiring Xia et al. [38], we give the following approach:
5.4  AN APPROACH TO TFM-ATTRIBUTE 
DECISION-MAKING PROBLEMS
Suppose that we have a decision-making problem given in the TFM-
environment. Let A = {a1, a2, …, am} be set alternatives to and C = {c1, c2, 
…, cn} be set of criteria. To evaluate the suitability of the alternatives ai 
with regards to cj, information that the alternative ai satisfies the attribute cj 
needed to be provided. Then, the TFM-decision matrix R = (rij)mxn should 
be constructed. In order to find the ranking of the alternatives, the follow‑
ing algorithm is given as follows:

Trapezoidal fuzzy multi-aggregation operators  129

of the expert considering the characteristic of the alternative ai with 
regards to cj based on Table 5.1 as follows:

Example 5.1

ware to make his business more modernized. For this, the head man‑
ager considers several criteria (or attributes) such as
c1: financial profit
c2: installation cost
c3: technological contribution
c4: speed up matters
The set of criteria is C = (c1, c2, c3, c4). Their weight vector is w = (0.3, 
0.2, 0.4, 0.1)T. Five suppliers to be considered and their set is Y = (y1, 
y2, y3, y4, y5).

5.6  CONCLUSION
In this chapter, we investigated multiple attribute group decision-making 
(MAGDM) problems in which the attribute values are given in the form of 
trapezoidal fuzzy information. Based on the notion of mean aggregations, 
a new type of mean aggregation operators called Archimedean norms 
operator for aggregating trapezoidal fuzzy information was introduced. 
Afterward, a solution algorithm was proposed to access a solution for 
multi-criteria decision-making problems. Finally, an illustrative example 
was given to verify the developed approach and to show its usefulness 
and efficiency. In the future, we plan to apply Archimedean norms to 
trapezoidal hesitant fuzzy numbers, triangular hesitant fuzzy numbers, 
trapezoidal intuitionistic fuzzy multi-numbers and neutrosophic fuzzy 
multi-numbers.

6.1  INTRODUCTION
Operational research (OR) is a scientific approach to problem-solving and 
decision-making that uses statistical analysis [1], mathematical modeling 
[2], and optimization techniques to help individuals and organizations 
make better decisions. It was developed during World War II [1939-1944] 
by Watt and Rowe [3] to manage scarce resources. Safitri et al. [4] proposed 
the dual simplex method to solve goal programming problems. Braglia et al. 
[5] introduced a stock diffusion theory for inventory control problems. 
Recently, Al‑Salih and Bohner [6] proposed the time scales approach for 
solving quadratic programming, which is an efficient and precise approach 
to this problem. OR has many other applications, but in this chapter, we are 
only discussing the inventory problem in the paragraph below.
Inventory models are an important application of OR techniques. An 
inventory model calculates the ideal inventory level for a business to 
meet consumer demand [7], inventory cost [8], and restocking time [9]. 
Additionally, we can also observe that the inventory control model has sev‑
eral applications, such as automotive [10], manufacturing [11], maintenance 
and repair [12], logistics and supply-chain [13], food and beverage [14], 
healthcare [15], aerospace and defense [16], construction [17], education 
[18], and so on. Table 6.1 provides a short overview of the different applica‑
tions of inventory control in various fields and highlights the main advan‑
tages and characteristics of each method.
Figure 6.1 and Table 6.1 can help readers better understand the con‑
cepts discussed in the chapter and visualize how inventory control works 
in practice.
After reviewing the introduction, it is evident that there are several 
gaps in the study of classical inventory problems. These gaps have moti‑
vated us to explore recent trends and advancements in uncertain inven‑
tory ­management systems. Our goal is to provide insights into evolutionary 

Recent trends and advancements in uncertain inventory management   139
methods commonly described by inventory theory and highlight key aspects 
of management, such as inventory and supply chain management with 
uncertain scenarios. By doing so, we aim to help researchers and students 
gain a thorough understanding of fuzzy inventory models (FIMs) through 
a single review chapter, which will aid in the discovery and encouragement 
of advancements in the fuzzy inventory management field.
To achieve this goal, our review article comprehensively surveys impor‑
tant aspects of FIM. We discuss the existence of inventory theory and its 
sub‑areas in real‑life problems and current challenges under uncertainty in 
inventory management.
In the subsequent sections of the chapter, we organize the flow as ­follows: 
Section 6.2 provides insight into the demerits of the classical inventory 
model. Sections 6.3 and 6.4 provide an overview of the FIM and the related 
research that has already been done, along with the latest extensions, 
respectively. We summarize the contribution made by various researchers 
on these models in a tabular form. Finally, in Section 6.5, we present a sum‑
mary of our conclusions regarding inventory management.
