6.2  THE DISCUSSION OF CHALLENGES  
OF THE CLASSICAL INVENTORY MODEL
In this section, we will be discussing the limitations of the Challenges and 
classical inventory models.
Valliathal and Uthayakumar [22] investigated the effects of an economic 
order quantity (EOQ) model on non‑instantaneously deteriorating com‑
modities related to time‑dependent selling rates, partial backlogging, and 
price variations all taken into account. Research verifies the existence and 
uniqueness of the optimal solution and examines the solution’s sensitivity 
to different inputs. The managerial ramifications of these findings are sub‑
stantial. Stochastic demand patterns can be added in place of deterministic, 
using both quantity discounts and payment delays can be implemented. 
Variables including changing prices, backorders, and demand over time are 
Figure 6.1  Pictorial representation of Table 6.1.

Recent trends and advancements in uncertain inventory management   141
standard inclusions in classical inventory models. It has been noticed that 
uncertainty can have a major effect on these factors, rendering the classical 
inventory model useless for predicting the best possible outcomes.
Sicilia et al. [23] examined deterministic inventory systems for constantly 
degrading items. The research assumes demand swings and follows a power 
pattern, allowing and backlogging shortages. Inventory management considers 
holding, backlog, ordering, depreciation, and purchasing costs. The research 
recommends two choice variables to minimize inventory cycle cost. Sicilia 
et al. [23] explored price variations, partial backlogging, and time‑dependent 
selling rates, as considered by Valliathal and Uthayakumar [22]. Ordering, 
storage, backlog, degradation, and purchase costs were examined. Because 
the uncertainty affects these variables, the conventional inventory approach 
may not reliably anticipate optimal solutions.
The paper by Shaikh et al. [24] presents an inventory model that incorpo‑
rates price, stock dependence, fully backlogged shortage, and inflation. The 
model describes the demand function as dependent on both the price and the 
stock, with demand during shortage periods dependent solely on the price 
of the product. The product price is dependent on different markup rates. 
The model considers deterioration as a non‑instantaneous process that starts 
after the item stock in the retailer’s inventory has been held for some time. 
Shortages are allowed and fully backlogged. This results in a constrained 
optimization problem that was solved using Lingo 10 software. To demon‑
strate and validate the inventory model, the researchers used a numerical 
example with different markup rates. The results of a sensitivity analysis were 
analyzed to identify inventory parameters alteration. The parameters used 
by Shaikh et al. [24] differ from those used by Valliathal and Uthayakumar 
[22] and Sicilia et al. [23]. Considering additional parameters, such as price, 
stock dependence, fully backlogged shortage, and inflation, which were not 
previously considered by the aforementioned authors, this uniqueness sets 
this book apart from other research articles in the field. The variables used in 
classical inventory models include price, stock dependence, fully backlogged 
shortage, and inflation.
The proposed EOQ model by Khurana et al. [25] assumes that each item 
in an order is in excellent condition. However, production systems cannot 
always rely on this assumption because of things like process deteriora‑
tion. Taking into account the time‑dependent and linear nature of prod‑
uct demand, the link between the demand rate, production rate, and the 
time‑dependent degradation rate of products, Khurana et al. [25] provide 
an economic production quantity model for deteriorating items. The model 
accounts for partially delayed shortages and assumes an infinite supply of 
storage space for objects that have totally degraded before being thrown 
away. To mimic backordering and lost sales, backlogging is used only when 
it is profitable to do so. To minimize overall average cost and live up to 
market demands and expectations, the proposed inventory policy optimizes 
production run time, production amount, and shortage period. Production 

142  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
cycle time, production volume, and shortfall duration are typical inputs in 
classical inventory models. However, conventional inventory models fre‑
quently fail to reliably forecast optimal solutions because of the significant 
level of uncertainty related to each of these factors.
In conclusion, inventory models are a set of mathematical strategies used 
to optimize stock on hand, purchase orders, and reorder points to meet 
consumer demands at the lowest possible cost. In some cases, in a decision‑ 
making process where there are multiple factors to consider, some of which 
may be difficult to quantify, fuzzy logic can provide a more flexible and 
nuanced approach than an inventory model. That is why, fuzzy logic can 
be better than inventory models because it can take into account a wider 
range of factors and can incorporate subjective or qualitative information. 
Therefore, the fuzzy theory was first introduced by Lotfi A. Zadeh [26] in 
his 1965.
6.3  THE INTRODUCTION OF THE 
FUZZY INVENTORY MODEL
Lately, Dubois and Parade [27] introduced the fuzzy inventory model. 
Fuzzy logic determines the ideal inventory levels that minimize inventory 
expenses and meet service standards. In economics [28], engineering [29], 
and management [30], the fuzzy inventory model has been improved to han‑
dle inventory management uncertainty and imprecision. Considering the 
common fuzzy inventory models such as Fuzzy Economic Order Quantity 
(FEOQ) [31], Fuzzy Just-In-Time (FJIT) [32], Fuzzy Production Inventory 
Model(FPIM) [33], Fuzzy Multi-Item Inventory Model(FMIIM) [34] and so 
on. These are examples of popular types of fuzzy stock inventories. Hence, 
fuzzy inventory models are used to handle the uncertainty and imprecision 
that exist in inventory management.
As we all know the FIM is a critical function for any business that deals 
with physical products. Recent trends and advancements in uncertain 
inventory management have focused on improving accuracy and ­efficiency 
in forecasting demand [35], managing inventory levels, and minimizing 
stockouts. Overall, these trends and advancements have greatly enhanced 
the ability of businesses to manage their inventory with greater accuracy 
and efficiency in the face of uncertainty. In below Table 6.2 and Picture 
6.2 provides a short overview of some of the major contributions to the 
FIM.
Figure 6.2, can help readers better understand the concepts discussed 
in the chapter and visualize how fuzzy inventory control operates in real-
world scenarios.

Recent trends and advancements in uncertain inventory management   143
6.4  SOME MORE RECENT TRENDS AND 
ADVANCEMENTS IN UNCERTAIN 
INVENTORY MANAGEMENT
In this section, we will be discussing recent trends and advancements in 
inventory models. Additionally, we have considered uncertainty as a signifi‑
cant parameter and discussed its effects.
Indrajitsingha et  al. [41] examined FIM with fixed degrading items, 
stock‑dependent demand rate, and shortages under fully backlogged con‑
ditions. Assuming the number of conditions, including one inventory with 
no lead time, full backlog allowance, infinite replenishment rate, but finite 
size, stock‑dependent finite time horizon, demand rate, and no repair of 
deteriorated items. All inventory‑related parameters were assumed to be 
hexagonal fuzzy numbers during the development of the suggested model 
in the fuzzy setting. A numerical example shows that the Signed Distance 
Method reduces the fuzzy model optimal cost outcome, compared with 
the crisp.
Saranya and Varadarajan [42] introduce inventory models often face 
uncertainty, which can stem from vagueness in the underlying assumptions. 
For instance, Saranya and Varadarajan [42] assumed constant demand and 
time, while allowing for shortage costs and fuzziness in holding, ordering,

both crisp and fuzzy models was analyzed and compared in Rajput et al.’s 
[38] study. The authors compared one‑parameter (deficiency) fuzzy models 
to two‑parameter (ample) crisp models. Some of the new parameters the 
authors have introduced to the crisp model are deterministic and probabi‑
listic forms of demand, variable lead time, inflation, partial backlogging, 
variable production rate, permissible delay, defective rate, remanufactur‑

The model was subsequently subjected to fuzzy approaches, which led to 
improved results like minimum overall cost and maximum profit.
Nagamani and Balaji [43], in last, suggested inventory models often suf‑
fer from uncertainty due to their inherent fuzziness, and adopting a fuzzy 
approach can bring us closer to reality. In their recent paper, Nagamani 
and Balaji [43] proposed a hazy inventory system that can accommodate 
shortages, leading to a complete backlog. They used fuzzy triangular num‑
bers to estimate carrying costs, backorder costs, and inventory costs and 
made certain assumptions, such as the demand for the goods being con‑
stant, a consistent timetable, and allowing for cost‑cutting, while fuzzily 
accounting for the costs of holding, ordering, and shortfall. This study 
aims to clarify the genuine profit function using the Graded Maximum 
Integration Value Method. The authors use arithmetic to demonstrate 
crisp and fuzzy modeling.
The literature analysis on FIMs involving stock‑dependent demand rate 
and fixed degrading items with shortages under fully backlogged conditions 
addresses uncertainty and fuzziness provides realistic results than crisp 
models. The Signed Distance Method and Graded Mean Integration Value 
Method Fuzzification inventory rules to minimize overall cost or maximize 
profit. The cycle assumes constant demand and time, zero lead time, totally 
backlogged shortages, finite time horizon, infinite replenishment rate but 
with finite size, and no repair of deteriorating items. In the crisp model, 
they added inflation, demand kinds, partial backlogging, lead time, manu‑
facturing rate, and retailer and supplier costs. These authors’ FIMs may 
increase inventory control and management accuracy.
6.5  CONCLUSION
This chapter critically reviews and classifies the current approaches and 
methodologies developed by researchers and scientists for modeling and 
analyzing inventory management systems in diverse sectors, such as health‑
care, supply chain, and routing problems. Furthermore, recent trends and 
advancements in inventory management systems in uncertain environments 
are also discussed. Drawing upon the findings of the literature review, we 
propose an integrated research framework that is applicable to current and 

146  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
future research endeavors. In the near future, we plan to conduct a litera‑
ture review on fuzzy extension theories, such as Neutrosophic, Pythagorean, 
Intuitionistic, and others. Our rationale for this is that we believe fuzzy the‑
ory alone is insufficient for effectively handling uncertainty. Keeping this in 
mind, we intend to undertake a comprehensive literature review on fuzzy 
extension theory.

7.1  INTRODUCTION
Operations research is a multidisciplinary field that leverages expertise 
from applied mathematics, computer science, and systems engineering [1]. 
Zachariah et al. [2] have noted that it encloses a wide range of techniques for 
improving decision‑making in real‑world problems [3]. Initially, the opera‑
tions research field was developed to improve military equipment during 
World War II, but since then, it has evolved and now applies to advanced 
analytical models to tackle logistical challenges in various industries. In 
operations research, decision‑making involves identifying the problem, and 
determining relevant decision criteria and constraints, by analyzing pos‑
sible outcomes using mathematical models and analytical techniques [4]. It 
describes the discipline as encompassing a broad spectrum of methods used 
to find the optimal values of decision variables subject to constraints, such 
as minimizing costs or maximizing profits [5].
The field of operations research offers a range of techniques that can be 
applied to decision‑making environments characterized by varying degrees 
of certainty, uncertainty, and risk in real‑life problems [6]. Knowledge of 
these environments enables the selection of a quantitative approach for 
decision‑making that can be utilized across multiple domains, including 
healthcare, finance, transportation, energy, and logistics. Optimization 
techniques, such as the simplex method [7], theory of games [8], assignment 
problem [9], the dual revised simplex method [10], integer linear program‑
ming [11], inventory [12], project evaluation and review technique/critical 
path method (PERT/CPM) [13], and many more, can aid in solving complex 
problems and make better decisions. Despite the benefits of optimization 
techniques, identifying some barriers exists within its sub‑areas as Azlan 
et  al. [14] examine techniques and approaches to enhance the efficiency 
and effectiveness of the simplex method. However, for large‑scale prob‑
lems, the simplex method may not be efficient. In their works, Kapliński 
and Tamošaitiene [15] and Faudzi et  al. [16] explored the use of game 

Comparative study on critical path method and project evaluation  151
theory in construction engineering, as well as in optimizing student–teacher 
­matching and course scheduling in education through assignment prob‑
lems. However, they also highlighted the limitations of game theory and 
assignment problems, which often rely on certain assumptions that may not 
accurately reflect real‑world situations. Further, Salookolaei and Nasseri 
[17] faced computational complexity in large datasets, as it requires more 
computational resources and time compared with other methods, especially 
for problems with numerous constraints and variables on working with a 
dual simplex method that uses duality results to solve grey linear program‑
ming problems with greater efficiency and accuracy.
Accordingly, Rieck et  al. [18] and Bruccoleri et  al. [19] employed the 
techniques of mixed integer programming and inventory problem, respec‑
tively. However, the accuracy and completeness of the input data used in 
both techniques can impact the reliability of the results obtained. The reli‑
ability of the outcomes in mixed‑integer linear programming relies heavily 
on the precision and completeness of the input data. Similarly, for inventory 
problems, incomplete data can lead to inaccurate inventory records and 
negatively impact the performance of the supply chain. Hence, ensuring the 
accuracy and completeness of the input data is crucial for obtaining reliable 
results in both techniques.
By observing the above limitations in the sub‑areas of operations research 
working under numerous real‑life problems, complex projects typically 
require a specific sequence of tasks and activities having interdependency 
between them. Complex projects require rigorous project planning and 
scheduling to manage all activities and resources. To resolve such cases, 
network analysis techniques like the CPM and PERT have proven to be 
useful in real‑life problems as they can effectively manage and control the 
complexity of large‑scale projects [20]. These techniques can help to iden‑
tify critical paths, optimize resource allocation, and provide a framework 
for effective project management. Therefore, CPM/PERT remains a valu‑
able tool in the arsenal of techniques used in operational research to tackle 
complex problems in various fields, including construction, engineering, 
and management [21]. Our primary objective is to review the application 
of CPM/PERT in the construction field under conditions of both certainty 
and uncertainty. CPM/PERT are scheduling methods that were originally 
designed to plan and analyze construction projects by coordinating cost 
and time criteria through a network of related activities [22]. According 
to Kelley et al. [23], CPM/PERT are beneficial tools for managing vari‑
ous types of projects, not only limited to construction, engineering, facility 
maintenance, research, and development.
In the next sections, this chapter reviews some state‑of‑art related to the 
construction field by observing various parameters under certainty and 
uncertainty using CPM/PERT techniques. This review aims to show dif‑
ferent developments in using CPM/PERT techniques to optimize better 
solutions in a certain environment of the construction field as observed 

152  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
in Section 7.2. Further, Section 7.3 identifies the observation using CPM/
PERT technique in the construction field under an uncertain environment 
followed by a conclusion and references.
7.2  LITERATURE SURVEY
This section is divided into two subsections, Section 7.2.1 discuss the back‑
ground study on classical CPM/PERT Section 7.2.2 discusses the identified 
challenges under CPM/PERT techniques in the construction field and the 
limitations while working in a certain environment, and Section 7.2.3 dis‑
cusses the working fuzzy CPM/PERT that is represented in Figure 7.1 and 
further Section 7.3 reviews the advancements and developments in con‑
struction field followed by observation and conclusion.
7.2.1  CPM/PERT: a background
CPM is considered a convenient tool that provides schedules for monitoring 
the development of project activities [23]. According to Snyder [24], CPM, 
also known as critical path analysis, was created in the 1950s by DuPont 
Corporation and Remington Rand Corporation to provide an accurate esti‑
mation of project cost and time. The Polaris project, initiated by the U.S. 
Navy in 1958 [25], led to the development of the PERT, which is one of 
the most widely used techniques for visualizing various project scheduling 
scenarios because of its great complexity and ambiguity. PERT concepts 
served as the foundation for CPM, and both approaches have developed 
concurrently adhering to the same planning paradigm [26]. The primary 
distinction between the two techniques makes use of a probabilistic distri‑
bution to distinguish between single‑time estimates and three‑time estima‑
tions [27]. The optimal statistical computation for project completion time 
might be obtained by combining CPM and PERT [28], which would enable 
the focus on critical activities and would allow non‑critical activities to 
become critical [29].
CPM/PERT techniques were developed for basic managerial functions 
of planning, scheduling, and controlling [22]. CPM/PERT was originally 
Figure 7.1  CPM/PERT environmental description.

Comparative study on critical path method and project evaluation  153
intended for construction project planning, to employ network‑related 
activities and analyze the representation of tasks that need to be completed 
in the given project, by coordinating optimal cost and time criteria [30]. 
The following subsections will discuss the application of CPM/PERT tech‑
niques in the construction industry.
7.2.2  Classical CPM/PERT under construction
For the literature review, several researchers’ works were examined to 
understand the workflow for the model development. Owing to an increas‑
ingly competitive environment, construction projects have become increas‑
ingly complex and dynamic due to the evolving requirements of technology 
and financial structures. In the past, traditional tools such as CPM/PERT 
were commonly used to manage construction projects, which followed a 
structured process. The underlying concept of minimizing total project 
duration was the basis for the development of CPM/PERT scheduling tech‑
niques, but now, due to the emergence of new technologies, construction 
projects demand a more flexible and adaptable approach to meet the chang‑
ing demands of the industry. According to Zhou et al. [31], project sched‑
uling entails several crucial challenges, including resource allocation, risk 
analysis, and scheduling fast track, as depicted in Figure 7.2. Table 7.1 sum‑
marizes the studies in the literature for a better understanding.
Based on the challenges, Joshi and Patil [32] found that analyzing the 
resource leveling in a construction project using CPM increased project 
length simultaneously increasing the project duration. CPM helps to plan, 
schedule, and manage construction projects by creating logical linkages 
between project activities. It is deterministic and ignores project activity 
uncertainty. Thus, probabilistic estimates are laborious as Liu [33] exam‑
ines the uses of PERT in construction risk analysis for risk management 
and performance improvement. In the study, it was discovered that PERT 
is a comprehensive framework for controlling, project planning, monitor‑
ing, and construction risk assessments. Additionally, Zareei [34] proposed 
a project scheduling model using CPM for the construction of a biogas 
Figure 7.2  Identified challenges during the construction application.

154  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
plant to demonstrate the CPM approach application in project scheduling 
for the renewable energy sector. The findings showed without any delays, 
by obtaining the minimum completion time.
As an alternative, heuristic methods of resolving the resource allocation 
problem have been suggested based on the resources, scheduling, and envi‑
ronments. As project resource‑scheduling issues have come to the fore, even 
though CPM/PERT exists, in such cases, artificial intelligence approaches, 
such as Genetic Algorithm (GA), have been used to implement CPM and 
PERT processes because of the complexity and difficulty. Using dynamic 
CPM and PERT networks, Calp and Akcayol [35] proposed a novel method 
for optimizing project scheduling tasks. The use of GAs illustrates the value 
of their suggested approach through a case study and contrasts the out‑
comes with those attained using alternative optimization techniques.
The lack of specificity in a situation with numerous possible outcomes 
due to insufficient information about the event’s result is referred to as 
uncertainty [36]. These uncertain situations, commonly known as risk 
analysis can be handled by the simulation technique. By integrating the 
probable value of each activity as a crucial value, according to Barraza [37] 
evaluation Monte Carlo Simulation has the advantage of producing realistic 
estimation results. Liu and Li [38] developed a risk management strategy

that is connected to project activities that have an impact on the entire proj‑

Further, Wali and Othman [39] proposed a risk management approach for

study and to compare its results with those obtained from traditional sched‑
ule analysis.
During the past decade, the fast‑tracking delivery method has become 
a popular alternative to the traditional sequential method due to its time‑­
saving feature. Fast‑tracking can affect the development process conven‑
tionally, despite its advantages. Barwa [41] compared the CPM/PERT in 
project planning, to identify which method is more suitable for fast‑tracking 
project delivery and Ba’Its et al. [42] proposed a combination of PERT and 
CPM for project schedule development, to enhance the accuracy of project 
scheduling and facilitate fast‑tracking project delivery. Recently, Ramdani 
et  al. [43] conducted a meta‑analysis study to evaluate the performance 
of three project management methods  –  CPM, PERT, and earned value 
analysis (EVA) – in controlling time and cost in fast‑tracked construction 
projects to identify the strengths and weaknesses of each method, as well as 
to provide insights into the best practices for project managers to effectively 
control time and cost in fast‑tracked construction projects.
Based on the literature survey, the problems identified under classical 
CPM/PERT are depicted in Figure 7.3.
In real‑world scenarios, upon reviewing the literature, uncertainty plays 
a critical factor in construction projects. However, estimating project dura‑
tion becomes challenging due to incurring risk events and associated con‑
sequences uncertainty. In today’s competitive world, completing projects 
on time without delays, addresses the problems of vagueness in real‑world 
decision‑making environments, Zadeh [44] proposed fuzzy set theory, 
Figure 7.3  Classical CPM/PERT problems.

156  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
which plays a significant role in various fields of science and technology, 
expanding the domain of decision‑making in industrial systems [45]. Fuzzy 
set theory is extensively used in linear programming, game theory, goal 
programming, dynamic programming, transportation models, and nonlin‑
ear programming [46].
7.2.3  FUZZY CPM/PERT: a background study
Chanas and Radosinski [47] and Prade [48] established the fuzzy network 
planning problem with the central idea, as the representation of non‑­
deterministic durations of operations using fuzzy numbers. The development 
of the fuzzy approach in network project analysis, with methods like the 
fuzzy CPM and fuzzy PERT proposes the use of fuzzy number sets to model 
the activity times. Several researchers, such as Chanas and Kamburowski 
[49], Dubois and Prade [50], Buckley [51], Chanas [52], Gazdik [53], Mares 
[54], Tsujimura et al. [55], McCahon and Lee [56], Han et al. [57], Chanas 
and Zielinski [58], Nasution [59,60], Slyeptsov and Tyshchuk [61], and Liang 
and Han [62] presented the utilization of fuzzy method in network analysis.
The incorporation of fuzzy logic in fuzzy CPM and fuzzy PERT is used 
to handle uncertainty and imprecision in project management. Fuzzy logic 
is a mathematical framework that can handle uncertainty and imprecision 
by assigning degrees of membership to different values. In traditional CPM 
and PERT methods, project activities are assumed to have ­deterministic 
durations and are represented as single‑point estimates. However, in real‑­
world projects, fuzzy CPM and fuzzy PERT can also provide more reliable 
estimates of project duration and help to make better project decisions. The 
proposed literature was used to identify and determine the implementation 
considering the effect of vagueness in the completion of individual con‑
struction works.
7.3  TRENDS IN CONSTRUCTION MODEL BY USING 
CPM/PERT UNDER UNCERTAIN ENVIRONMENT
The fuzzy set theory plays an important part in the construction industry; 
it has been studied over the last 50 years. The various literature is studied 
to get an overview of the factors responsible for the construction industry 
using the fuzzy logic set theory. Table 7.2 summarizes the studies in the 
literature for better understanding.
One of the earliest attempts to solve the resource leveling problem was 
made by Crandall [63], who defined the core idea of resource leveling as a 
process that allows available resources to activities that satisfy a collection 
of project and time‑related criteria. Further many studies, such as Chang 
and Ibbs [64] developed a fuzzy expert system that incorporates multiple 
parameters to make informed decisions in resource scheduling. This system 

Comparative study on critical path method and project evaluation  157
offers a flexible and accurate approach, which can improve project out‑
comes and increase efficiency in the construction industry. As an extension 
to the work, Chang and Crandall [65] proposed an expected possibility 
algorithm based on fuzzy set theory that handles uncertain and imprecise 
information in construction resource allocation. The algorithm considers 
inherent uncertainties providing a more realistic and accurate approach to 
construction resource allocation. The algorithm also helps decision‑makers 
assess the risks associated with resource allocation decisions and identify 
areas for improvement.

158  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
Further, risk management is a crucial aspect of project management, 
and every project is subject to various types of risk. However, Mazlum and 
Güneri [66] proposed a fuzzy logic‑based approach using fuzzy CPM and 
fuzzy PERT to estimate expected time, variance, and project activity comple‑
tion, along with a risk assessment model to analyze potential project risks. 
Similarly, Soni et al. [67] also involved a fuzzy logic‑based approach that 
integrated CPM and PERT techniques to evaluate and assess the risks associ‑
ated with various activities in a construction project. The proposed approach 
provided a more comprehensive and accurate risk assessment than tradi‑
tional methods and generated results that can be utilized to develop effective 
risk management strategies. According to earlier research, as summarized 
by Lootsma and Lootsma (1997) [68], fuzzy logic is used to enhance tra‑
ditional CPM/PERT planning by considering fuzzy activity durations and 
resources with a variety of heuristic and meta‑heuristic methods. To analyze 
crucial activity in CPM/PERT networks, Yakhchali [69] path presented a 
path‑sorting technique using fuzzy activity time values. The research sets out 
to identify the most optimal critical activities and pathways and calculates 
the probability criticality degree of activities using an efficient method based 
on a path enumeration technique by obtaining real‑world project networks.
Late completion of a project can have detrimental effects on a company, 
including penalties and customer dissatisfaction. In such situations, project 
managers can use additional resources to bring the project back on track, 
called as crashing in project management. However, since crashing a proj‑
ect involves additional costs, the decision to crash needs to be made cost‑­
effectively. To choose the best course of action, the trade‑off between the 
penalty cost and the crashing cost must be considered. To address this chal‑
lenge, Rahman et al. [70] proposed a fuzzy crashing algorithm that incorpo‑
rates fuzzy logic into the CPM/PERT networks to improve the accuracy and 
reliability of crashing analysis. This approach allows more realistic and flexible 
project planning and decision‑making in uncertain environments, particularly 
in large‑scale construction projects. The paper presents numerical examples 
and comparative analyses to demonstrate the effectiveness and advantages 
of the proposed fuzzy CPM/PERT approach. Similarly, Galagali [71] investi‑
gated the critical issue faced by project managers in delivering projects within 
the shortest possible time, while maintaining maximum quality and minimiz‑
ing cost. The study implies that project crashing may impact quality, and an 
actual construction project was investigated to determine time, cost, and qual‑
ity trade‑off and highlights managerial insights and major issues.
In further problems, time‑cost trade‑off (TCTO) analysis attempts to 
shorten the project duration, effectively and efficiently. By identifying the 
ideal combination of activities and building techniques, TCTO analysis aims 
to reduce the overall project cost without lengthening the project’s comple‑
tion time. Leu et  al. [72] suggested a flexible strategy that blends using 
GA fuzzy set theory to resolve the uncertainties related to activity duration 
and expenses. They looked at how unpredictable activity durations affected 

Comparative study on critical path method and project evaluation  159
project direct costs were represented by fuzzy numbers. Like this, Abbasnia 
et al. [73] solution for resolving the TCTO using fuzzy logic theory by con‑
sidering uncertainties that impact the direct and indirect cost of a construc‑
tion project. To give a trade‑off between time and overall cost they used a 
non‑dominated sorting genetic technique.
Recently, Likewise, Habibi [74] survey estimated the time and cost of proj‑
ect activities using a fuzzy‑based methodology. The proposed method was 
used to estimate the implementation cost of a five‑story building construc‑
tion project in Tehran, Iran. The study shows that the proposed fuzzy‑based 
methodology reduces the impact of uncertainties on results and improves 
the accuracy of project cost estimation. Therefore, the approach holds on 
providing more accurate and realistic project cost estimation results.
Based on the literature survey, the problems identified under fuzzy CPM/
PERT are depicted in Figure 7.4.
As per the literature mentioned above, fuzzy CPM/PERT was developed 
to address some of the limitations in construction, compared with clas‑
sical CPM/PERT, particularly due to its inability to handle uncertainty 
and  imprecision in activity duration estimates. The primary motivation 
behind the development of Fuzzy CPM/PERT was to provide a more flex‑
ible and robust framework that could accommodate imprecise data and 
reflect the inherent uncertainty in project planning and scheduling, i.e., 
real‑world complexity, flexibility, improved accuracy, decision‑making, etc.
7.4  CONCLUSION
Classical CPM/PERT are planning and scheduling techniques that are 
widely used in construction, which solve many problems for better solu‑
tions in large‑scale projects. Due to their inefficiency in dealing with 
Figure 7.4  Fuzzy CPM/PERT problems.

160  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
impression or ambiguity in project time estimates, classical CPM and PERT 
have ­limited success in the ongoing environment of the construction phase. 
In such cases, the aim is to perform a thorough review of the literature on 
fuzzy extension theories. Our motivation for this review stems from our 
belief that fuzzy theory, on its own, does not adequately address the chal‑
lenges of managing uncertainty. Thus, we plan to conduct a comprehensive 
analysis of fuzzy extension theories, keeping this perspective in mind. The 
results of the study observations have demonstrated that fuzzy CPM and 
fuzzy PERT are widely employed in many projects‑related challenges, not 
only involving construction but immensely used in many applications. The 
study concluded that CPM and PERT approaches combined with fuzzy sets 
offer a fertile foundation for future researchers in the construction field.

Chapter 8
A short literature on linear 
programming problem 
under uncertainty
Shubham Kumar Tripathi and Ranjan Kumar
8.1  INTRODUCTION
Operations research (OR) is a field of advanced applied mathematics that 
was developed during World War II (1939-1944) by Watt and Rowe [1]. 
It plays an important role in decision-making and problem-solving across 
many sectors and industries. According to Hillier and Lieberman [2], 
“Operations research is a scientific approach to solving decision problems, 
which involves the application of mathematical methods and models, and 
the use of computers to aid in decision‑making.” Some researchers [2], [3] 
have proposed various methods to solve linear programming problems 
(LPP). Powell [4] introduces a stochastic approach to solve the dynamic 
assignment problem for truckload motor carriers. Williams and Haley [5] 
suggest using transportation to solve the problem, which involves trans‑
forming the LPP into a transportation problem. Ehrgott et.al [6] offer a 
simplex primal-dual algorithm for multicriteria LPP to obtain the optimal 
solution. Cachon and Netessine [2] propose game theory as a useful tool 
for analyzing supply chain problems lastly, Abdullah and Abidin [3] recom‑
mend using linear programming (LP) with a single objective function to 
maximize profits for the red meat production problem. Hence, operations 
research is a field that deals with solving complex problems. Within the 
OR, there are various branches, and LP is one of the most important ones. 
If you want to explore linear programming further, please read the below 
paragraph.
LP is a mathematical modeling method normally used in OR to solve 
optimization problems. In 1947, G. Dantzig [1] introduced the LP model 
to solve the optimization problem. It involves optimizing a linear objective 
function while the constraints are also linear. OR uses LP to discourse a vari‑
ety of issues in different sectors, including transportation [8], logistics [9], 
and resource allocation [10]. Currently, LP is a popular technique utilized 
in numerous fields, such as economics [11], management [12], and so on.

Based on the aforementioned introduction, it becomes evident that there

changes and improvements in FLP. This chapter helps authors to learn both 
classical and FLP models through a single review chapter. Additionally, it 
focuses on basic parts of classical and FLP models, such as the sequencing 
problem [18], queueing theory [19], and Integer programming [20], and so 
on.
The main points covered in this review article are as follows: a concise sur‑
vey of significant aspects of the LP model. Discussion on the identification of 
LPPs and their sub‑areas in real‑life scenarios is characterized by uncertainty. 
Review of existing methodologies for solving LPP, including the graphical 
method [21], revised simplex [22], dual simplex [22], simplex method [1], and 
among others.

In this chapter, the subsequent sections are organized as follows. Section 8.2 
presents a discussion of the different types of classical LP ­models. Section 
8.3 explores some more recent trends and advancements in uncertain vari‑
ables. Section 8.4 introduces the concept of the fuzzy LPP. Finally, Section 
8.5 ­concludes the chapter.
8.2  DISCUSSION OF THE DIFFERENT  
TYPES OF CLASSICAL LPP MODELS
LP is a type of mathematical modeling technique that optimizes the linear 
objective function subject to a set of linear constraints. Several types of lin‑
ear programming models are commonly used, such as the graphical method 
[21] in LPP, the simplex method [7], dual simplex [22], revised simplex [7], 
and so on.
The Graphical method [21] is a useful tool for solving LPPs graphically, 
especially when they have two variables. LV Kantorovich introduced this 
method in 1939 [21]. However, the graphical method cannot solve prob‑
lems with multiple variables and constraints. To address this issue, George 
Dantzig [1] introduced the simplex method in 1947. In, 1954, C.E. Lemke 
Figure 8.1  Pictorial representation of Table 8.1.

168  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
[22] proposed another approach, the dual simplex method. However, none 
of these methods can handle the degeneracy and infeasibility in some cases. 
Therefore, an improved alternative, the revised simplex method, was devel‑
oped. The revised simplex method is a powerful and widely used algo‑
rithm for solving LPPs, and it remains an important tool in the field of 
optimization. Although many researchers have various methods, we can’t 
discuss them all in this manuscript. Hence, we will focus only on the sim‑
plex method. We will discuss the simplex method in detail in the following 
section of this chapter. Moreover, we will review of other authors and high‑
light the advantages and disadvantages of their approaches. For example, 
we will provide a detailed discussion on the use of a bi-objective optimiza‑
tion algorithm by Moradi et al. [23].
Moradi et al. [23] propose a column generation approach for resolving 
the bi‑objective multi‑commodity minimum cost flow problem (MOMCFP) 
that builds upon the bi‑objective simplex method and Dantzig–Wolfe 
decomposition. The technique starts by optimizing the problem regarding 
the first objective, which is a single‑objective multi‑commodity flow prob‑
lem, and utilizing Dantzig–Wolfe decomposition. Subsequently, it itera‑
tively advances from one non‑dominated extreme point to the next, finding 
the entering variables with the maximum ratio of improvement in the sec‑
ond objective to the first objective’s deterioration, similar to the bi‑objective 
simplex method. The performance of the algorithm is evaluated on various 
directed bi‑objective network instances with varying characteristics and 
numbers of commodities. Moradi et al. [23] attempted to address network 
problems in their article, recognizing that networks are highly impacted by 
uncertainty. It is well‑known that current LP approaches struggle to handle 
uncertain or imprecise information and cannot provide sufficiently robust 
solutions. Consequently, the existing LP models fail to offer optimal solu‑
tions due to the significant level of uncertainty present in network models. 
In line with this, Moradi et al. [23] focused on the bi‑objective problem. 
Similarly, Bonkoungou et al. [24] also worked on bi‑objective LPP. We have 
endeavored to present their contributions in the following paragraph for 
your reference.
Bonkoungou et al. [24] introduced a novel method for solving bi‑­objective 
LPP using the simplex method. The paper provides an overview of the fun‑
damental concepts of multi‑objective optimization and network flows, along 
with a concise literature review of existing work on MOMCFP. The algo‑
rithm’s key feature lies in its ability to discover the optimal solution without 
disrupting the network structure of the problems at hand. Network flow 
problems, particularly minimum cost network flow problems, are versatile 
optimization problems capable of modeling various real‑world scenarios. 
Computational results indicate that as the number of arcs in a fixed number 
of nodes increases, the average CPU time exhibits a significant rise. Hence, 
the CPU time primarily depends on the number of arcs in the network being 

A short literature on linear programming problem  169
optimized. Notably, the algorithm is limited to linear objective functions 
with several variables subject to a set of linear equality or inequality con‑
straints. Future developments should explore addressing nonlinear objec‑
tive functions and constraints. Additionally, the practical application of the 
algorithm to real‑time decision problems remains a challenging endeavor 
that requires further exploration. Bonkoungou et  al. [24] investigated in 
their article to address the problem of telecommunication networks. It is 
well‑known that telecommunication networks are highly susceptible to 
the impact of uncertainty. Consequently, existing linear LP approaches are 
incapable of effectively handling uncertain or imprecise information, and 
they also fail to provide robust solutions. As a result, the current models are 
unable to yield optimal solutions due to the substantial level of uncertainty 
inherent in telecommunication network models.
In conclusion, Moradi et al. [23] proposed a column generation approach, 
building upon the bi‑objective simplex method and Dantzig–Wolfe decom‑
position, to solve the bi‑objective MCMCFP. Similarly, Bonkoungou et al. 
[24] introduced a simplex method‑based approach to solve bi‑objective LPP, 
focusing on the challenges posed by telecommunication networks. When 
faced with decision‑making processes involving multiple factors, some of 
which may be challenging to quantify, fuzzy logic offers a more adaptable 
and nuanced alternative to LP models. This is because fuzzy logic can cover 
a broader range of considerations and contribute to qualitative or subjective 
data. As a result, fuzzy logic surpasses LP models by taking into account 
a wider array of factors. The concept of fuzzy theory was originally intro‑
duced by Lotfi A. Zadeh [25] in 1965.

8.3.2  Introduction of FLP problems
The FLP model, initially presented by Zimmermann [28]. According to 
Dubois and Prade (2008) [29], “Fuzzy linear programming is a mathemati‑
cal programming methodology that allows for uncertain or imprecise data 
to be represented as fuzzy sets, and for the optimization of a fuzzy objective 
function subject to fuzzy constraints.” FLPP allows for the modeling and 
analysis of complex systems that involve uncertain or imprecise parameters, 
to handle the real-world problems. It helps us find better and more adapt‑
able ways to solve problems in the real world. In below table 8.2 and Figure 
8.2 we have discussed significance of FLP.
Incorporated within the chapter is Figure 8.2, which presents a graphical 
depiction of the components and processes involved in FLP. 
In the below section, we provide a short summary of the fuzzy simplex 
method (FSM). By understanding the basic concepts of the FSM, authors 
can appreciate its possible applications and benefits.
8.4  SOME MORE RECENT TRENDS AND 
ADVANCEMENTS IN UNCERTAIN
This section focuses on exploring the latest trends and advancements in 
FLP with a particular emphasis on the utilization of the Simplex method. 
Furthermore, the consideration of uncertainty as a crucial parameter is 
addressed, and the effects of uncertainty are thoroughly discussed.
Mansur Hassan [35]: LP is a widely used tool in operations research, 
­offering efficiency and simplicity in solving real‑life problems. However, 
in many cases, managers and decision‑makers lack precise information 
for the parameters used in optimization models. To address this issue, 
FLP provides a flexible approach that can effectively handle such situ‑
ations. This paper introduces a proposed simplex method for solving 
parametrized LP with imprecise resources. The main objective of this 
paper is to present a more comprehensive simplex method for solving 
FLP problems with fuzziness at the right‑hand side (RHS).
Liu and Shi [36]: Introduced a fuzzy stratified simplex method to 
solve complete stratified fuzzy multi-objective linear programming 
(FMOLP). The authors demonstrate that the obtained optimal solu‑
tion is also the fuzzy efficient solution of the FMOLP problems. 
The proposed method offers the advantage of directly obtaining the 
fuzzy optimal solution using  the extended primal simplex method. 
Furthermore, the authors prove that this fuzzy optimal solution is also 
the fuzzy efficient solution of the original FMOLP problem.
Tuffaha and Alrefaei [37]: This paper introduces a fully FLP problem 
with piecewise linear fuzzy numbers (PLFN‑n) and proposes a solution

method based on extending the simplex method. Tuffaha and Alrefaei 
[37] proposed a method that generalizes the conventional simplex 
algorithm and yields identical results when applied to crisp LPP. The 
use of PLFN‑n expands the range of problems solvable by this method, 
including transportation or supply chain problems with fuzzy data. 
Applying the method to an FFLP problem with PLFN‑n preserves

Behera et al. [38]: In this research paper, Behera et al. [38] propose a 
methodology to solve FTP using linear fractional fuzzy numbers. 
The objective functions are represented by triangular fuzzy numbers 
(TFN). The main objective of this paper is to find optimal solutions to 
FTP using the simplex method, with TFN used to represent the costs 
of the objective function.

Overall, this chapter contribute to the field of operations research by pro‑
viding methods and techniques for solving FLP problems with various types 
of fuzziness.
8.5  CONCLUSION
This chapter has provided a detailed analysis and discussion of the fuzzy 
simplex method. The aim of this manuscript to analyze the challenges of 
the classical model. By focusing on the simplex method and drawing upon 
a comprehensive literature review, we have provided valuable visions into its 
theoretical fundamentals, algorithmic implementation, and advancements. 
The FSM presents opportunities for further research and development 
in area of LPP. This chapter serves as a valuable resource for researchers 
and practitioners seeking to optimize LPPs. Near future, our objective is 
to conduct an extensive analysis of the literature on fuzzy extension theo‑
ries, which involves Neutrosophic, Pythagorean, and Intuitionistic theories, 
among others. Our primary motivation for this review is rooted in our 
conviction that fuzzy theory alone is insufficient for effectively managing 
uncertainty. With this viewpoint in mind, we intend to undertake a thor‑
ough evaluation of fuzzy extension theories.

Chapter 9
A note on translation of 
bipolar-valued fuzzy sets in 
Sheffer stroke MTL-algebras
T. Oner, I. Senturk, and A. Rezaei
9.1  INTRODUCTION
When constructing a mathematical model, it is crucial to simplify the expres‑
sion as much as possible by using the least number of axioms or operations. 
Tarski tackled this issue in his study of Abelian groups, where he found that 
a single axiom characterizes them based on the divisor operator [1].
Following Tarski’s approach, Oner and Senturk introduced the Sheffer 
stroke operation to reduce basic algebras to Sheffer stroke basic alge‑
bras, which play a critical role in numerous logics such as many-valued 
Łukasiewicz logics, non-classical logics, and fuzzy logics [2].
More recently, Esteva and Godo introduced MTL-algebras in [3], which 
have been the subject of many theorems aimed at strengthening their theo‑
retical framework [4,5]. For instance, Vetterlein demonstrated that most 
MTL-algebras can be embedded in the positive cone of a partially ordered 
group [4], and he also established that MTL-algebras are integral, commu‑
tative, bounded, and prelinear residuated lattices [5].
MTL-algebras have emerged as the most basic residuated structures that 
can induce all algebras from their residua and continuous t-norms, making 
them crucial in working with fuzzy logics and related structures [6].
In addition to their logical efforts, Senturk proposed a reduction for 
MTL-algebras solely based on the Sheffer stroke operation, which he called 
Sheffer stroke MTL-algebras [7]. Later, Senturk and Oner introduced the 
“very true” operator on Sheffer stroke MTL-algebras [8].
The concept of fuzzy sets was first introduced by Zadeh in 1965 [9]. 
Since then, fuzzy set theory has been widely applied in mathematics and 
other fields. There are various extensions of fuzzy sets, including intuition‑
istic fuzzy sets, interval-valued fuzzy sets, vague sets, and bipolar-valued 
fuzzy sets.
The notion of bipolar-valued fuzzy sets was introduced by Lee in 2000 
[10], as an extension of fuzzy sets with membership degree range [−1, 0]. 
Since then, several generalizations of bipolar-valued fuzzy sets have been 
proposed, and their applications to logical algebras have been studied 
extensively. For instance, Jun and Song [11] introduced bipolar fuzzy 

178  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
subalgebras and bipolar fuzzy closed ideals in BCH-algebras in 2008, while 
Jun and Park [12] introduced bipolar fuzzy regularities, bipolar fuzzy regu‑
lar subalgebras, bipolar fuzzy filters, and bipolar fuzzy closed quasi-filters 
in BCH-algebras in 2009. Lee and Jun [13] introduced the notion of bipo‑
lar fuzzy a-ideals of BCI-algebras in 2011, and Jun et al. [14] introduced 
bipolar fuzzy CI-subalgebras, bipolar fuzzy ideals, and (closed) bipolar 
fuzzy filters in CI-algebras in 2012. In 2014, Muhiuddin [15] introduced 
bipolar fuzzy KU-subalgebras and bipolar fuzzy KU-ideals in KU-algebras, 
while Senapati [16] introduced the notion of bipolar fuzzy BG-subalgebras 
in BG-algebras in 2015. Recently, Sabarinathan et al. [17] introduced the 
notion of bipolar-valued fuzzy ideals and bipolar-valued fuzzy H-ideals of 
BF-algebras in 2016 and 2017, respectively.
Bipolar-valued fuzzy sets have found applications beyond logical algebras 
and have been extended to bipolar fuzzy translations in various algebraic 
structures. In 2009, Jun et al. [18] introduced the notions of bipolar fuzzy 
translations and bipolar fuzzy S-extensions of a bipolar fuzzy subalgebra 
in BCK/BCI-algebras. In 2012, Sardar et al. [19] extended the concept to 
bipolar-valued fuzzy translations and bipolar-valued fuzzy S-extensions of 
a bipolar-valued fuzzy sub-semigroup (bi-ideal) in semigroups.
These studies demonstrate the importance of bipolar-valued fuzzy sets 
and their applications to various algebraic structures, which can help to 
develop more effective algorithms for solving complex problems in various 
fields.
In this study, we put forward the concept of bipolar fuzzy translation 
and bipolar fuzzy S-extension of a bipolar fuzzy subalgebra in a BCK/BCI-
algebra, along with an investigation of their properties.

Application of the greedy

Souhail Dhouib, Saima Dhouib, and Aida Kharat
10.1  INTRODUCTION
The transportation problem (TP) is one of the famous problems in combi‑
natorial optimization. It deals with shipping products from source to desti‑
nation nodes. The objective of the TP is to determine the minimal shipping 
cost while satisfying supply and demand limits. These parameters cost, 
demand and supply are not always defined as precise numbers due to the 
incertitude of the industrial environment in real‑life applications. To get 
around this circumstance of vagueness the concept of fuzziness was intro‑
duced by Zadeh (1965) and if the parameters of the TP are represented by 
fuzzy numbers, then it becomes a fuzzy transportation problem (FTP).
According to Ebrahimnejad and Verdegay (2018), FTP can be classified 
to four classes as follows:
•	 FTP‑Type 1: only the transportation cost is considered a fuzzy number.
•	 FTP‑Type 2: both the supply and the demand are considered as fuzzy 
numbers as well as decision variables.
•	 FTP‑Type 3: transportation cost, supply and demand quantities are 
fuzzy.
•	 FTP‑Type 4: all model parameters (cots, supply and demand) as well 
as decision variables are presented with fuzzy numbers.
This research paper presents the first adaptation of the novel heuris‑
tic Dhouib‑Matrix‑TP1 (DM‑TP1) to solve the triangular FTP where all 
parameters are fuzzy (FTP‑type 3). In the first step, triangular fuzzy num‑
bers are converted to crisp numbers using the score function. Then, the 
DM‑TP1 heuristic is extended to solve the FTP. The paper is organized as 
follows. In Section 10.2, a literature review of TP under a fuzzy environ‑
ment is presented. In Section 10.3, the fundamental concept of triangular 
fuzzy and suitable ranking functions is given. In Section 10.4, the proposed 

Application of the greedy Dhouib-matrix-TP1 method  205
method is discussed. In Section 10.5, a stepwise application of a numerical 
example is illustrated with a comparison of the generated result to other 
methods from the literature, and in Section 10.6, the paper is concluded.
10.2  RELATED WORK
In recent years, several techniques are adopted for solving the TP in a fuzzy 
environment. A novel ranking method to convert Fermatean fuzzy number 
to crisp number in full FTP is designed (Sahoo, 2021). The unbalanced TP 
where all parameters are presented as octagonal fuzzy number is optimized 
(Muthukumara et al., 2021). A new procedure FTP based on the study of 
the median transforms this problem into an exquisite value problem to find 
a minimal transport cost (Srinivasan et al., 2021). A new approach to solve, 
compare and estimate the costs of triangular, heptagonal and pentagonal 
numbers with the application of the Lagrange polynomial is used for FTP 
(Mhaske and Bondar, 2020). Later, a new method to solve fuzzy trapezoi‑
dal TP is presented (George et al., 2020). A conventional one‑point model 
to optimize fuzzy trapezoidal TP is proposed (Bisht and Srivastava, 2019). 
A new approach is established for the minimization of transport costs of 
FTP by classification of trapezoidal fuzzy numbers and it is presented by 
Kasilingam and Subramanian (2018). A new distance ranking technique 
for converting the PTF to umber is net worth is proposed by Karthikeyan 
and Mohamed (2016). A new fuzzy demand and supply ranking method 
with trapezoidal fuzzy numbers is used for FTP (Balasubramanian and 
Subramanian, 2018). A new λ‑cut technique based on the classification 
with fuzzy numbers is proposed for the resolution of FTP (Hunwisai and 
Kumam, 2017). A new approach for solving FTP by converting this prob‑
lem into a linear programming problem based on the Monte Carlo method 
by the use of triangular fuzzy random numbers is applied (Bondar and 
Mhaske, 2017). A new process fuzzy variable classification approach with 
trapezoidal numbers to convert FTP to a net problem is proposed by Malini 
and Ananthanarayanan (2016). A new approach for solving FTP where cost 
coefficients are fuzzy numbers while demands and supplies are net num‑
bers is presented (Khalaf, 2014). A new FTP resolution approach based 
on the heptagonal classification of fuzzy numbers using the ambiguity and 
value parameters is established (Rathi and Balamohan, 2014). A simplex 
algorithm named Arsham Khan is applied for solving FTP with imprecise 
requirements (Samuel and Gani, 2011). A new method to optimize FTP 
based on optimal more‑for‑less solution with mixed constraints based on 
zero fuzziness is applied in strategic decision‑making notions (Pandian and 
Natarajan, 2010a). A new method named fuzzy zero point is used for solving 
FTP with trapezoidal fuzzy number parameters (Pandian and Nagarajan, 
2010b). A novel ranking function to convert trapezoidal number to crisp 
one for TP is developed (Abbasbandy and Hajjari, 2009).

206  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
A new technique for classifying and comparing triangular fuzzy numbers 
to find a good solution for the FTP which is based on determining the area 
of a trapezium, a rectangle and a centroid at prominent places using the cut‑
ting method is used (Deshmuk et al., 2021). The TP where supply, demand 
and cost are presented as triangular fuzzy numbers is solved through a new 
method (Srinivasan et al., 2020). A new algorithm for solving the fuzzy TP 
using a new ranking approach based on the total integral value (Sam’an 
and Farikhin, 2020). The TP with the modern zero suffix method under 
a fuzzy environment is solved (Dinagar and Keerthivasan, 2020). A solu‑
tion for FTP with triangular fuzzy numbers implicated ranking function 
to solve problems project schedules, assignment problems and network 
flow problems using imprecise transportation costs is developed (Kumar 
and Subramanian, 2018). The resolution of FTP with a new technique on 
the lexicographical order of triangular fuzzy numbers with three‑objective 
functions is proposed (Ebrahimnejad, 2017). A new method is established 
for the resolution of the FTP with triangular fuzzy numbers to reach a 
minimal cost of transporting products with a capacitive network with four 
fuzzy numbers which are supply, demand, capacity and cost is developed 
(Srinivasan et al., 2016). An improved two‑step method for solving the FTP 
with non‑negative triangular fuzzy numbers by using fuzzy arithmetic to 
convert the FTP to linear programming with fuzzy costs and constraints 
net then decompose it to transform it to net bounded TP (Ebrahimnejad, 
2015). A new method proposed for FTP resolution with parameters (cost, 
availability and demand) in symmetric triangular fuzzy numbers according 
to Vogel’s algorithms is proposed (Nareshkumar and Ghuru, 2014).

tion, we will introduce some details about the fuzzy concept inspired from 
the work of Bellman and Zadeh (1970) and Ross (2004).

10.4  PROCEDURE OF DHOUIB‑MATRIX‑TP1 (DM‑TP1)
The DM‑TP1 is a greedy heuristic based on several rules to orient the 
research towards a good initial basic feasible solution. The heuris‑
tic DM‑TP1 is one of several methods proposed in the concept of DM. 
Considering the optimization of the Travelling Salesman Problem in certain 
and uncertain environment, the new heuristics DM‑TSP1 and DM‑TSP2 
are proposed based on the concept of DM (Dhouib, 2021d–l; Dhouib and 
Dhouib, 2021; Dhouib et al., 2021; Dhouib, 2022a; Dhouib et al., 2022). 
Correspondingly, another novel heuristic is designed (through the idea of 
DM) entitled DM‑AP1 to solve the assignment problem (Dhouib, 2022b; 
Dhouib, 2023a; Dhouib and Sutikno, 2023). Moreover, for large instances 
new metaheuristics are proposed via the theory of DM, such as DM‑3 and 
DM‑4 introduced, respectively (Dhouib et al., 2021; Dhouib and Zouari, 
2023) and (Dhouib, 2022c; Dhouib and Pezer, 2022; Dhouib, 2023b).
The novel heuristic DM‑TP1 is introduced firstly (Dhouib, 2021a) to solve 
TP with crisp parameters. Moreover, DM‑TP1 is enhanced to optimize 

208  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
TP under the neutrosophic domain (Dhouib, 2021b) and trapezoidal FTP 
(Dhouib, 2021c). Figure  10.1 depicts the nine steps of DM‑TP1, where 
step 1, step 2 and step 9 are executed only once and the other steps are 
repeated n + m iterations. A stepwise application of DM‑TP1 will be given 
in the next section. For more details on the theory of DM‑TP1, we suggest 
the reader to refer (Dhouib, 2021a–c).

nodes (W1, W2 and W3). Figure 10.2 depicts the corresponding param‑
eters (cost, supply and demand) for this problem represented as a triangular 
fuzzy number.

10.5.1  Iteration 1
Compute the (Average‑Min) function for each row, place the result in the 
AMSR at the last column, repeat that for each column and put the result in 
the AMDC (Figure 10.4). Next, identify the highest element (with the value 
3 in column 2) among AMSR and AMDC and find the minimal element in 
the column (1.5 at position x12). Hence, affect the smallest value between 
supply 7.5 and demand 10.5 (which is 7.5) and discard the saturated param‑
eter (row 1).
10.5.2  Iteration 2
Similarly, compute the values of AMSR and AMDC (Figure 10.5). Then, 
select the highest element (with the value 2.5 in column 2) among AMSR 
and AMDC and find the minimal element in the column (3.5 at position 
x22). Next, affect the smallest value 3 and discard the saturated parameter 
(column 2).
Figure 10.4  Select the smallest element in column 2.

Application of the greedy Dhouib-matrix-TP1 method  211
10.5.3  Iteration 3
Once more, compute the values of AMSR and AMDC (Figure 10.6). Then, 
select the highest element (with the value 1.5 in column 3) among AMSR 
and AMDC and find the minimal element in the column (2.5 at position 
x23). Next, affect the smallest value 3 and discard the saturated parameter 
(row 2).
Figure 10.5  Discard row 1.
Figure 10.6  Select the smallest element in column 3.

212  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
10.5.4  Iteration 4
Another time, compute the values of AMSR and AMDC (Figure 10.7). Then, 
select the highest element (with the value 0.5 in row 3) among AMSR and 
AMDC and find the minimum in the column (4.5 at position x31). Next, 
affect the smallest value 5.5 and discard the saturated parameter (column 1).
10.5.5  Iteration 5
Yet again, compute the values of AMSR and AMDC (Figure 10.8). Then, 
select the highest element (with the value 0.0 in row 3) among AMSR and 
AMDC, and find the minimum in the column (5.5 at position x33). Next, 
affect the smallest value 3 and discard the saturated parameter (row 3).
All columns are discarded then a minimal transportation solution is gen‑
erated (see Figure 10.9) and its corresponding cost is calculated as follows:

10.6  CONCLUSION
In this paper, the novel heuristic DM‑TP1 is enhanced for the first time 
to solve the TP where cost demand and supply are represented as trian‑
gular fuzzy numbers. DM‑TP1 is a very rapid method of viewing that 
it needs only (n + m) iterations to generate the optimal or near‑optimal 
transportation plan. DM‑TP1 is effective and easy to understand and 
can be applied for solving real‑life problems. The results generated from 
the numerical comparisons prove the performance of DM‑TP1 compared 
with the existing techniques. Further research will deal with the applica‑
tion of DM‑TP1 on other fuzzy membership functions and aims to invent 
a new ranking function.

11.1  INTRODUCTION: SHORTEST PATH 
PROBLEMS IN A FUZZY ENVIRONMENT
A mathematical method for analyzing relationships between objects and 
resolving network issues is called graph theory (node). Due to its fundamen‑
tal characteristics and a wide variety of applications, shortest path problem 
(SPP) plays a crucial role in combinatorial optimization. In communication, 
computer networks, manufacturing systems, and transportation, finding the 
shortest paths is crucial. The path’s weight will serve as a representation of 
the amount of time needed to convey something from one end to the other, 
or from the source to the destination. By reducing the distances between 
some pairs of suppliers and destinations and by speeding up some of the 
routes, the transmission’s efficiency can be raised. The entire cost should 
be lower to meet the demands of the speedup, and some cost is required 
to reduce travel time by enhancing the road conditions for faster travel [1]. 
Every SPP has a set of specifications for the supplier and leaf node that cor‑
respond to a quantity such as time, the quantity of goods a truck may carry, 
or the duration of a break. The limitations on the resource will be given in 
the form of intervals that restrict the range of values it can consider at each 
node along the path. It is possible to encrypt SPP using a complete graph as 
an assignment problem. The system of transport management must be able 
to provide the quickest route between a specific source node and the ter‑
minal node. Instead of indicating distances in terms of geography, the arc 
lengths are stimulated to reflect the time or expense of transportation. For 
a setting with uncertainty, the use of fuzzy numbers is a viable strategy. The 
defuzzification function is used to convert fuzzy numbers into crisp num‑
bers, which are then frequently employed in optimization techniques. SPP 

220  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
is not bound by a geometric distance. The trip time between cities, despite 
it being fixed, might be represented by a fuzzy variable. Since practically 
all communication and transportation networks include unpredictably 
weighted arcs, it is impossible to create clear graphs using this information. 
The fuzzy shortest path problem was first successfully solved by Dubois 
and Prade. Finding the SP to the directed graph and its primary format 
incapable of representing the circumstances where the value of the detached 
function should be is the most significant combinatorial optimization chal‑
lenge. The most significant combinatorial optimization problem is to iden‑
tify the SP to the directed graph and its basic structure that is incapable of 
depicting situations in which the result of the disconnected function cannot 
be determined just by the choice of each single arc.
In a murky circumstance, Rosenfeld [2] explained the idea of graph the‑
ory. Researchers also defined fuzzy graph operations and types, such as 
complements of fuzzy graphs, regular fuzzy graphs, etc. Intuitionistic fuzzy 
sets (IFS) were described by Atanassov [3] as a generalized fuzzy set with 
active and inactive membership categories. The fact that a fuzzy set’s ele‑
ments have a membership value distinguishes it from a normal set. Zadeh 
made the discovery [4]. Shannon et Atanassov developed the theory of the 
intuitionistic fuzzy graphs [5]. Jun and Lee [6] presented cubic ideals in 
semigroups. Jun et al. [7] presented a publication of closed cubic ideals and 
cubic‑subalgebras in BCK/BCI algebras. Jun et al. [8] presented BCI alge‑
braic cubic q‑ideas. Cubic structures were used to apply BCI algebras and 
other ideals [9,10].
In a directed graph with each edge’s cost represented by a trapezoidal 
intuitionistic fuzzy number (TrIFN), the most generalized form of trap‑
ezoidal fuzzy numbers (TrFNs), consisting of degree of acceptance and 
degree of rejection, Baba [11] presented an algorithm to find an intuition‑
istic fuzzy shortest path (IFSP). To find the IFSP, the suggested method 
employs the induced intuitionistic trapezoidal fuzzy order weighted geo‑
metric (I‑ITFOWG) operator (IFSP). Gani and Jabarulla [12] used an intu‑
itionistic fuzzy number rather than a fuzzy number to denote each arc 
length when describing the shortest path in a directed graph. In a network 
flow problem, Meenakshi and Kaliraja [13] determined the shortest path in 
interval‑valued fuzzy networks. As an extension of the spherical fuzzy set, 
Jun and Khan et al. [14] suggested the idea of the cubical fuzzy set. In 2013, 
Cuong [15] debated using picture fuzzy sets. In 2014, Das and De [16] 
presented the shortest path problem in an intuitionistic fuzzy environment. 
Based on interval valued, fuzzy SPP, Elizabeth and Sujatha [1] discussed 
fuzzy number matrices.
Yager [17] established a class of non‑standard Pythagorean fuzzy subsets 
with membership grades of pairs (a, b) meeting the condition. Khorsandi 
et al. [18], suggested the simplest method for determining the fuzzy shortest 

An approach toward shortest path problems  221
path (fuzzy shortest distance) between the source node and the destination 
node. The method required less time since it only considered the nodes that 
needed to travel to the destination node and excluded all other nodes from 
consideration.
Broumi et al. [19] presented a paper describing the use of the Dijkstra 
method to resolve the interval‑valued neutrosophic shortest path problem. 
An interval type 2 fuzzy set with the shortest path problem was given by 
Dey et al. [20].
Fermatean fuzzy sets (FFSs), which may handle ambiguous information 
more easily throughout the decision‑making process, were introduced by 
Senapati and Yager [21,22]. They defined basic operations over ferma‑
tean fuzzy sets. They compare and contrast the drawbacks of Pythagorean 
fuzzy sets with intuitionistic fuzzy sets. They focused on the complement 
operator of fermatean fuzzy sets and established the fundamental set of 
operations for fermatean fuzzy sets. Another thing that was looked at was 
an Euclidean separation between two fermatean fuzzy sets. They devel‑
oped a fermatean fuzzy weighted product model to handle the problem of 
multi‑criteria decision‑making.
Jan et al. [23] made some progress in fuzzy graph theory. An original pro‑
posal for a T‑spherical fuzzy graph is presented as a characteristic shared 
by fuzzy graphs, intuitionistic fuzzy graphs, and picture fuzzy graphs. This 
idea’s uniqueness, importance, and significance are demonstrated by show‑
ing some findings, giving examples, and employing graphical analysis. In this 
section, terms like a T‑spherical fuzzy subgraph, a complement of T‑SFG, 
and degree of T‑SFG are defined and their attributes are described. Sahoo 
[24] proposed specific score functions for the fermatean fuzzy TOPSIS tech‑
nique and the effective ranking of fermatean fuzzy sets on the basis of pro‑
posed score functions. The authors have proposed three newly improved 
ranking score systems for fermatean fuzzy sets. Mahmood et al. [25] dis‑
cussed about multi‑criteria decisions based on the cubic set. Thamizhendhi 
et al. [26] defined the concept of Fermatean fuzzy hypergraph.
A new matrix technique for minimal spanning trees with undirected 
interval‑valued neutrosophic graphs was suggested, as per Broumi et  al. 
[27]. Dey et al. [28] presented a genetic approach for tackling fuzzy short‑
est path issues with interval type‑2 fuzzy arc lengths. Solving shortest path 
problem using the Dijkstra algorithm in interval‑valued fuzzy sets (IVFs), 
Enayattabar et  al. [29] demonstrated a fuzzy Pythagorean environment. 
Using t‑norm operations, Kaur and Garg [30] developed generalized cubic 
intuitionistic fuzzy aggregation operators and applied them to group deci‑
sion‑making processes. Research works related to authors toward shortest 
path problems under various fuzzy environments are shown in Table 11.1.

222  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
11.2  REVIEW OF SOME ESSENTIAL CONCEPTS
Let us give a brief review of some essential concepts that are concerned with 
the present study.
	
i.	Fuzzy set: A fuzzy set is characterized by a membership function map‑
ping element of a domain, space or universe of discourse X to the unit 
interval [0, 1], i.e., A = {(x, μA(x); x ∈ X}, Here μA: X → [0, 1] is a mapping 
called the degree of membership function of the fuzzy set A and μA(x) is 
called the membership value of x ε X in the fuzzy set A. These member‑
ship grades are often represented by real numbers ranging from [0, 1].
	
ii.	Pythagorean fuzzy set: A Pythagorean fuzzy set (PFS) A on a 
universe of discourse X is a structure having the form as A =

	 ix.	Collection of fuzzy sets: Let X be a non‑empty set. A fuzzy set is 
defined by a function α: X → F, where F = [0, 1]. Denote FK as the col‑
lection of all fuzzy sets in X.
	
x.	Interval number: A closed subinterval d =  [d−, d+] of F where F = [0, 1] 
is called an interval number, where 0 ≤ d− ≤ d+ ≤ 1. The set of all interval 
numbers is denoted by [F]. Let X be a non‑empty set. A function β: 
X → [F] is called an IVF in X. Let [F]K denotes the set of all IVF sets 
in X. For each I ∈ [F]K and x ∈ X, I(x) = [I−(x), I+(x)] is called the degree 
of membership of an element x to I, where I−: X → F and I+: X → F are 
fuzzy sets in X, which are defined as lower fuzzy set and upper fuzzy 
set in X, respectively.
	 xi.	Interval‑valued fuzzy set: Let X be a non‑empty set. The cubic set on 
X is defined by:

denoted by Iˆ = <I, α>.
	 xii.	Internal cubic set: Let X be a non‑empty set. A cubic set Iˆ = <I, α> is 
said to be an internal cubic set if I−(x) ≤ α(x) ≤ I+(x), ∀x ∈ X.
	xiii.	External cubic set: Let X be a non‑empty set. A cubic set Iˆ = <I, α> is 
said to be an external cubic set if α(x) ∉ (I−(x), I+(x)), ∀x ∈ X.
	 xiv.	Cubic sets: Let X be a non‑empty set. Suppose Iˆ = <I, α> and Jˆ = <J, β> 
be the cubic sets in X. Then,

11.3  OBJECTIVES OF THIS WORK
The main objectives of this research are as follows:
	
1.	To define an arithmetic operations on CFN.
	
2.	To present a scoring function system for a Cubical fuzzy graph, as 
well as an algorithm for solving issues utilizing this idea.
	
3.	Test the suggested method using a real‑world example.
This chapter might inspire scholars to examine a variety of real‑world issues 
using the recommended algorithm.

An approach toward shortest path problems  227
11.4  ALGORITHM FOR THE PROPOSED METHOD
This section proposes a technique to find the shortest path from each node 
to its predecessor. This approach can be used to locate the shortest path in 
a network in real‑world situations.

11.6  CONCLUSION
Conventional SP problems need accurate arc weights, which are not usu‑
ally available in real‑world situations. The current study investigates the 
shortest path issue with cubical fuzzy arc costs. The SP issue was initially 
articulated in the cubical fuzzy environment. We compared the costs of 
several pathways using an existing enhanced scoring function, with their 
arc costs represented by CFNs. We described the cubical fuzzy shortest 
Figure 11.2  Shortest path of cubical fuzzy network.
Table 11.3  Shortest Path
Nodes  
No. (j)
di
Shortest Path from First Node 
to jth node
2
0.7, 0.4, 0.1
1 → 2
3
0.3, 0.5, 0.5
1 → 3
4
〈
〉
0.376, 0.15, 0.4
1 → 3 → 4
5
0.393, 0.105, 0.24
1 → 3 → 4 → 5
6
0.7,  0.4,  0.1
1 → 3 → 4 → 6

232  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
path optimality requirements for the SP issue under discussion using this 
enhanced scoring function. The standard technique has been expanded to 
compute the CF cost and related CFSP of the shortest path. This study 
develops an algorithm for determining the shortest path in a network with 
nodes that take the form of CFNs. The proposed methodology is being 
used to handle a numerical example where the shortest path in a net‑
work is determined among all possibilities. In the future, we plan to use 
the method to solve more intricate network problems with both positive 
and negative CF costs. The technique described for addressing SP issues in 
an interval‑valued cubical fuzzy (IVCF) environment may be expanded to 
solve them in a generalized cubical fuzzy environment [1,28]. Furthermore, 
the suggested approach for determining the IVCF shortest path between all 
pairs of nodes is deferred until the next research.
