12.1  INTRODUCTION
The 1944 publication of Theory of Games and Economic Behavior by 
Princeton University Press [1], authored by economist Oscar Morgenstern 
and mathematician John von Neumann, is regarded as a seminal work in 
the development of the interdisciplinary field of game theory. The appen‑
dix of the second edition contains his derivation of expected utility from 
his axioms (1947). Considering that each person possesses the same prob‑
ability, Von Neumann and Morgenstern employed objective probabilities. 
Neumann and Morgenstern assert that they can offer a theory of subjective 
probability, nevertheless. This chapter [2] examines the reliability analysis 
of power loom plants using interval‑valued intuitionistic fuzzy sets (IVIFS). 
Here, we outlined the power loom installation as an easily removable sys‑
tem with A (n) and B (m) mingled in series. The IVIFS [3] specified over 
the universe of discourse [0, 1] is presumed to be the reliability of unit Aʼs 
n constituents and unit Bʼs m constituents. In order to account for heredi‑
tary uncertainties in data gathering and dependability measurement of the 
hand‑loom configuration, the concluding system reliability is an IVIFS.
A few of the currently prevalent ambiguous regression models [4,5] entail 
different demands for the focus of the study, such as asserting that the sam‑
ples are balanced triangular fuzzy numbers or applying non‑negative bounds 
on the estimated coefficients. We advocate left‑right fuzzy regression in this 
chapter that can be used with diverse forms of datasets, which represents 
the fuzzy distance [6–8] – the average fuzzy distance between the observed 
and projected quantities as the averaged fuzzy failure, then trying to apply 
the mean faulty inaccuracy to the minimal to yield the regression increase 
coefficients. and incomplete scale between two left‑right fuzzy numbers. To 
evaluate how well the suggested method performs in comparison to other 
methods, we use two criteria: average fuzzy error [9] (comparative average 
fuzzy error based on partial order) and Sum of Squared Errors (SSE). In 
order to show the viability and broad applicability of the suggested method, 
we conclude by providing four different types of numerical examples.

Different forms of linear dodecagonal fuzzy number  235
The fuzzified matrix game, a two‑player zero‑sum match with a vague 
reward, is taken into consideration in this article [10]. He introduces 
three kinds of minimax steady state conceptions and studies their pecu‑
liarities for these games, which have been predicated on fuzzy maximal 
arrangement. As a series of bi‑matrix parameterized games with sharp 
payoffs, these stationary techniques [11] are first showed to be Nash 
equilibrium strategies. Furthermore, we analyze the value qualities of 
the fuzzy matrix game using the possibility and inevitability metrics. 
Additionally, we provide a numerical example to illustrate the utility of 
our approach”. This contribution [12] describes a general fuzzy trans‑
port problem model. Introduce octagonal fuzzy numbers and develop a 
new model to solve this problem. This is the case when defining octago‑
nal fuzzy number [13] rankings. It is possible to compare them and use 
it to transform fuzzy values. The fuzzy optimal value is the transport 
problem when solved with octagonal fuzzy numbers has many conse‑
quences optimal value than solve using trapezoidal fuzzy numbers. It is 
illustrated using a numerical example by Basirzadeh [3].
Given the significant exposure of various interval‑weighted fuzzy numbers 
in neothelic investigations, they investigated different policies of inter‑
val‑weighted pentagonal fuzzy numbers in conjunction with tuples mem‑
bership functions (MFs) in this chapter [14]. In order to explain the rigidity 
and polarity of pentagonal fuzzy structures, the concept of MF is also 
somewhat generalized to quadratic MFs. As a result, level sets were built for 
both linear and nonlinear MF. Alternative approaches and a selection pro‑
cedure, which are also components of this framework, were used to achieve 
defuzzification. The intelligence created is incorporated in game situations 
using his PFNʼs parameters, which ultimately opens up new possibilities for 
modeling real‑world issues and improves how parameter uncertainties are 
handled during the evaluation process.
Fuzzy number rankings play an important role in optimization tech‑
niques [15] such as mapping problems, transportation problems, project 
planning, network flow analysis, and uncertain environments in organi‑
zational economics. In this chapter, we present a new ranking technique 
based on hexagons and fuzzy numbers focused on triangles and rectangles. 
“This is how the new ranking method makes hexagonal fuzzy numbers 
crisp number”.
This chapter [16] presents a novel approach to solve a two‑person 
zero‑sum matrix game combining player fuzzy tactics and fuzzy payoffs. 
A pair of unique linear programming models, one for each player, are cre‑
ated using a fuzzy triangular optimization algorithm. An example using 
numbers is used to show every improved method for each player. It visu‑
ally displays a simulation study on numerous gameplay quality and player 
strategy‑related elements.

236  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
12.2  MOTIVATION
The building of mathematical models, complicated engineering problems, 
and problems with medical diagnostics, among other things, depend criti‑
cally on how ambiguity is perceived. There will now be a crucial question: 
What is the mathematical figure and how a Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number 
symmetric linear form would be, if one were to think about it? We truly 
endeavor to build this research piece from this perspective. Later, we devel‑
oped some more intriguing findings regarding the defuzzification methods 
of the symmetric linear form of a Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number.
12.2.1  Contribution
In this explorative study, we have perceptualized the notion of Dodecagonal 
Fuzzy Number in linear form. We have introduced the MFs of Linear 
Dodecagonal Number in symmetric and asymmetric forms, Interval‑Valued 
Linear Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number in symmetric and asymmetric forms. We 
have explored the α‑cut parametric representations of Linear Dodecagonal 
Number in symmetric and asymmetric forms, Interval‑Valued Linear 
Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number in symmetric and asymmetric forms. Various 
defuzzification methods are solved using dodecagonal fuzzy number. They are 
as follows: (i) Center of Area Method, (ii) Average of α‑cut method, (iii) Bounded 
Area Method, and (iv) Mean of Removal of the Bounded Area Method.
To meet the needs of modeling problems, complicated engineering prob‑
lems, problems with inconsistent information, problems oriented to medical 
diagnosis, and operations research problems, the exploration of this num‑
ber will be an answer to all the critical situations. The researchers found it 
interesting to explore the possibilities of the usage of fuzzy number which 
is in symmetrical and non‑symmetrical nature. In this scenario, we have 
taken an assignment problem in Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number domain, in 
which it is resolved using the defuzzification methods explored.
12.2.2  Novelties
Many forms of exploration of fuzzy numbers have been done by many 
researchers and academicians. In this paper, we have brought out major 
interesting facts and discoveries of a fuzzy number with 12 parameters. 
They are as follows:
•	 Introduced the linear forms of Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number with both 
symmetric and asymmetric nature.
•	 The graphical representation of all forms of Dodecagonal Fuzzy 
Number.

Different forms of linear dodecagonal fuzzy number  237
•	 Discovery of defuzzification methods like the Center of Area Method, 
Average of α‑Cut Method, Bounded Area Method, and Mean of 
removal of Bounded Area Method.
•	 Implementation of Assignment Problem.

12.7  LIMITATIONS OF THE WORK
The research work has been clearly done on studying the complete form of a 
number. The number taken for the study contains 12 parameters. The attri‑
butes can be well confined if the problem undergoes with 12 parameters. 
Real‑life problems which have lack information cannot be dealt with the 
proposed number. Finding a solution for such kind of problems becomes 
impossible (Tables 12.1–12.4).

Different forms of linear dodecagonal fuzzy number  253

Different forms of linear dodecagonal fuzzy number  255
12.8  FUTURE DIRECTION
The newly proposed linear and non‑linear forms of the number are left open 
for young researchers to apply and find a solution to problems in real‑time 
fields like engineering, prediction analysis, image processing techniques, 
neural network problems, stock market exchange prediction, production 
analysis prediction, and so on. The study gives way for many researchers to 
explore the newly found linear and non‑linear representation with symmet‑
ric and asymmetric properties in various real‑time problems for attaining 
better solutions.
12.9  CONCLUSION
We have explored the notion of deeply analyzing the characteristics and 
nature of a Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number. Furthermore, we have done 
an elaborative study on expanding the same number to Interval‑Valued 
Dodecagonal Fuzzy Number having a linear form. The α‑cut parametric 
representations with symmetric and asymmetric properties have also been 
studied. The deep study has led us to a clear understanding of a Dodecagonal 
Fuzzy Number and its behavior. Various defuzzification strategies have 
been found for the new number. The inventions and explorations of the 
new formula have been best illustrated by an example assignment problem 
with all defuzzification techniques.

13.1  INTRODUCTION
Cheng (1998) has proposed a ranking procedure for problems that 
are unreasonable. They have proposed a method with a co‑efficient of 
­variation which is an improvisation of Lee and Li’s method. Deng Feng 
Li et al. (2010) have introduced triangular intuitionistic fuzzy number. 
A ranking method called value and ambiguity index methods have been 
proposed having triangular intuitionistic fuzzy numbers as values. A com‑
parison has been done with the existing methods to prove the strength of 
the proposed method. Angela M. Coxe and Clifford A. Reiter (2003) have 
done an elaborative study on the patterns and symmetry of snowflakes. 
They also have concentrated on the growth of the snowflakes which are 
indeterministic in nature and the issue has been addressed in this regard. 
Salim Rezvani (2013) has considered the usage of trapezoidal intuitionis‑
tic fuzzy number in decision‑making. The belongingness and non‑belong‑
ingness values are calculated using the value index and ambiguity index. 
K. Dhurai and A. Karpagam (2016) have introduced the hexagonal fuzzy 
number membership function. The fuzzy arithmetic operations have been 
studied in detail. The α‑cut properties have been used in these arithmetic 
calculations. Ghadle and Pathade have found a solution to a transpor‑
tation problem with Generalized Hexagonal and Generalized Octagonal 
Fuzzy Number. A ranking method has been used using the proposed num‑
bers. Namarta et al., have solved uncertainty game problems using dode‑
cagonal fuzzy number. The crisp value has been changed into a fuzzy 
game problem and a solution is found. Jiuying Dong and Shu‑Ping Wan 
(2016) have introduced interval‑valued intuitionistic linguistic variables, 
and using these methods, they have introduced weighted arithmetic oper‑
ators and weighted geometric operators. These formulas are being used in 
the lexicographic method. Mohammed Shapique and Jesuraj (2017) have 
implied pentagonal intuitionistic fuzzy number to find a solution to fuzzy 
differential equations. The values for belongingness and non‑belonging‑
ness is calculated which is the significant importance of the chapter.

14.1  INTRODUCTION
In 1965, the theory for dealing the uncertainties which is the concept of 
fuzzy sets was introduced by Zadeh [4]. The theory of fuzzy topology was 
initiated by Chang [2] in 1968. The concept of vague sets was first devel‑
oped by Gau and Buehrer [3].

14.3  VAGUE TRANSPORTATION MODEL
A simplified presentation of a new computing procedure for solving the vague 
transportation problem was presented. To design the algorithm, we have 
described the vague arithmetic and numerical conditions in three different 
models through the vague approach. We have extended the initial basic feasi‑
ble solution and then used an existing optimality test called the stepping stone 
method to obtain the cost of transportation. To justify the proposed method, 
a few numerical experiments are given to show the effectiveness of the new 
model, and the solution is presented in the way of the vague bipartite graph.
14.3.1  Algorithm for solving the three different 
types of vague transportation models
Step 1: To solve the vague transportation problem.
	
a.	If it is Type I vague transportation problem, then calculate the de-
fuzzified value of each vague cost and replace all the vague cost with 
its de-fuzzified value to obtain the classical transportation problem.
	
b.	If it is Type II vague transportation problem, then calculate the de-
fuzzified value of each vague supply and demand unit and replace 
all the vague supply and demand units with its de-fuzzified value to 
obtain the classical transportation problem.
	
c.	If it is Type III vague transportation problem, then calculate the de-
fuzzified value of each vague cost, vague supply and vague demand 
unit and replace all the vague cost, vague supply and vague demand 
unit with its de-fuzzified value to obtain the classical transportation 
problem.

280  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
Step 2: To check the balance of a transportation problem, we use

variable on demand/supply and make it a balanced transportation

Step 3: To find the initial basic feasible solution, use the Penalty method 
as shown in Algorithm 14.1.
Step 4: Convert the transportation problem into the crisp transportation 
problem by using

Step 5: Test the optimality of the transportation problem by the stepping 
stone method as shown in Algorithm 14.2.
Step 6: Using the objective function, get the minimum transportation 
cost.
Algorithm 14.1
A penalty method to find an initial basic feasible solution:
Step 1: Consider the problem from Step 1 of the main algorithm.
Step 2: Calculate the difference between the minimum and the next min-
imum of the transportation costs and denote it as a penalty.
Step 3: In the row/column, corresponding to the maximum penalty, make 
the allotment in the cell having the minimum transportation cost.
Step 4: If the maximum penalty corresponds to: 
Case 1: If there exists more than one row, select the topmost row,
Case 2: If there exists more than one column, then select the left-most 
column.
Repeat Steps 3 and 4 until all the supplies and the demands are 
satisfied.
Algorithm 14.2
A stepping stone method to test the optimality condition:
Step 1: Consider the LP model problem from Step 4 in Algorithm 14.1.
Step 2: To find the optimal solution using the stepping stone method, use 
the below algorithm.

14.4  CONCLUSION
Vague sets being a generalization of fuzzy sets provide a possibility to 
­represent the membership and non-membership functions with the means of 
uncertainty. Though there are many transportation problems that have been 
studied with different types of input data, this research has investigated the 
solutions to transportation problems in a vague environment. Three ­different 
models in vague environment were considered in the study. The proposed 
algorithms concretely analyze the solutions to vague ­transportation prob‑
lems to obtain the minimum transportation cost, and one of the solutions is 
obtained and represented by the vague bipartite graph.

Chapter 15
A fuzzy Ishikawa diagram 
to represent and evaluate 
the quality of education
W. Ortega-Chávez, F. Campos-Solórzano,  
E. J. Díaz-Zúñiga, C. M. Zacarias-Mercado,  
M. A. Flores-Romayna, and E. López-Navarro 
15.1  INTRODUCTION
One of the concepts of modern life that concerns almost every citizen on the 
planet is that of quality. This concept has had different definitions through‑
out history. At present, it is identified mainly with the results in production 
and services; however, daily, each one has an assessment of quality beyond 
these two aspects of contemporary human life.
Some formal definitions understand quality as full compliance with a 
set of pre-established requirements. Other definitions, including ISO 9000 
standards, define quality as customer satisfaction (ISO, 2006).
The conception of quality in this 21st century owes a lot to the Japanese 
school of this concept. It was the Japanese after World War II who absorbed, 
promoted, developed, and improved the ideas introduced by the Americans 
who were sent to the Asian country as quality advisers. Japan had lost the 
war and was a destroyed country that needed to be rebuilt but with low-
skilled labor.
Among the Japanese who received the American legacy of quality and 
who made very significant contributions is Kaoru Ishikawa, who was an 
industrial chemist, business administrator, and academic who is an essen‑
tial name to mention when talking about quality in the industry (Watson, 
2004). Among his contributions is the differentiation that he established 
between the Japanese and Western conceptions of work, due to cultural 
differences. The Japanese control of the total quality is characterized by the 
participation of all the members of the company to make it prosper, and 
for this end, they intervene from the senior managers to the lower-ranking 
employees. It is a less statistical total quality control than the Western style.
Also, K. Ishikawa designed easy-to-use tools in the field of industry, with 
the aim that they have simple but effective ways to measure the state of 
quality. These tools are based on the principle that a group of members 
of the company, those designated for it, will collectively and proactively 
analyze the factors that influence the quality of production, so that their 

290  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
positive modification contributes to the improvement of the quality of the 
product or service, following the principle of continuous improvement.
One such tool is the cause-effect diagram, also called the Ishikawa dia‑
gram, fishbone diagram, or herringbone diagram, named for its fishbone-like 
appearance. In this diagram, the “head of the fish” represents the problem 
to be solved, while the “bones” are lines that specify factors or causes that 
influence the problem. This is one of the seven basic quality tools.
There are other tools and other quality theories that can be extended 
to measure quality in education (Delahoz-Domínguez et al., 2022; Benty 
et al., 2023). However, the analogy between quality in the industry and 
education quality is at least non-trivial. This is because in the industry the 
objective is to satisfy the client’s expectation, but in education, it is neces‑
sary to define clearly the objective because the student is not a client. So, 
this is a controversial topic.
For example, in higher education worldwide, the idea has been estab‑
lished that the ranking of a university means that there is a higher quality 
in teaching; however, this is not necessarily true, because higher education 
centers have dedicated themselves to the publication of scientific articles, 
which does not mean that education quality is fulfilled in their classrooms.
Like the ISO 9000 standards that were drafted by the International 
Organization for Standardization, in the case of educational quality we 
will refer to what the United Nations documents say in the United Nations 
Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO):
Quality is a core element of education that not only has an impact on 
what students learn but also on the way they learn and on the benefits 
they obtain from the instruction they receive. The search for means to 
ensure that students obtain decent school results and acquire values 
and skills that allow them to play a positive role in their societies is a 
topical issue in the education policies of the vast majority of countries.
(UNESCO, 2005)
They also add:
Governments that are currently striving to develop basic education 
have to face the following challenge: ensuring that students stay in 
school long enough to finish their studies and equip themselves with 
the necessary knowledge to face a rapidly changing world. The evalua‑
tions carried out show that this is not being achieved in many countries.
In other words, one of the basic principles of quality education is that it be 
an education for all, which implies accessibility to education by all citizens 
who are willing to study; also, it must consist of equity, not discrimination, 
and equal opportunities (Nikulin & Nakonechnay, 2022; Shcherbak et al., 
2022). Once the student is in school or university, he/she must have all the 
necessary means for effective learning, both in knowledge and ethics.

A fuzzy Ishikawa diagram  291
As Ishikawa proposed its diagram to improve the quality of the company 
or industry, in this chapter, we propose an Ishikawa Fuzzy diagram that 
helps not only with the representation of knowledge and analysis of quality, 
but also for quantitative or qualitative evaluation of the status of quality in 
school, university, or teaching within a region or country.
The Ishikawa diagram has been used to represent knowledge of health 
issues in the clinical specialty or in education itself (Artyukhov et al., 2021; 
Cheong-Wong, 2011). However, to the knowledge of the authors of this 
chapter, we have not found the term “Fuzzy Ishikawa Diagram” except 
for a book chapter, where a fuzzy membership function is obtained auto‑
matically, with a computer program which we do not know if we have free 
access, where we need various mathematical knowledge beyond the simplic‑
ity of the original diagram (Dorodnykh et al., 2020).
Briefly, before proceeding, it should be remembered that fuzzy logic (FL) 
introduced by Zadeh, assigns truth values between 0 and 1 to logical prop‑
ositions to indicate gradations in semantics, unlike classical mathematical 
logic where a proposition is either true (truth value 1) or false (truth value 0) 
(Jyh-Shing et al., 1997; Klement et al., 2000). This is one way to deal with 
the uncertainty of information. In addition, that allows us to take advantage 
of the vagueness of natural language for the construction of models. That is 
why when we link Ishikawa diagrams with a theory as powerful as FL, this 
new tool is enriched, without implying a significant loss of simplicity. On the 
other hand, the diagram gains in utility and allows us to link with the ben‑
efits of the models that deal with natural language. The tool proposed by us, 
unlike the others, is simple to use and is also easily automatable if desired.
This chapter is divided into a section of preliminary concepts where the 
main notions of FL, the Ishikawa diagram, and quality in education are 
recalled. Next, the details of the proposed model are presented and illus‑
trated with an example. In the end, the conclusions are explained.
15.2  PRELIMINARY CONCEPTS
In this section of the chapter, we make a brief reminder of the theories used 
in the design of the proposed tool. First of all, the concepts of education 
quality that appear in the most recent scientific literature will be explained.
15.2.1  State of the art of educational quality
According to the Carriazo-Díaz et al. (2020): “For the Ministry of National 
Education, quality education allows the formation of integral citizens with 
a high formation in values, with respect for the good of others and in pur‑
suit of social principles such as coexistence and peace. It affirms that a qual‑
ity education generates great opportunities for progress for society itself 
and therefore for the entire country.”

292  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
According to Maya et al. (2019): “To achieve quality in education, seg‑
mentation must be avoided, and on the contrary, the role of integration of 
education in an unequal context that seeks to develop an educational proj‑
ect for social transformation should be strengthened.”
Morales-Gomez et al. (2019) propose a “quality principle” whose purpose 
is to “incorporate into their existence the policy of continuous improvement 
and innovation in search of excellence,” following the criteria that “Quality 
and excellence are more attitudes than results; that is why they integrate 
mistakes into the learning process.”
In the case of Peru, especially in higher education, Aroquipa et al. (2019) 
point out that: “growth in education has a reason, which is to achieve the 
general and growing well-being of students, where individuals and fami‑
lies can experience a continuous improvement in their living conditions, 
while society as a whole sees its possibilities, opportunities, and capabilities 
increase and expand. Faced with this, it cannot be denied that there are 
factors against it; that impede improving the quality of life of people. On 
the one hand, the global economic reality and, in turn, how Peru is inserted 
into it, on the other hand, the historical features of our society such as mar‑
ginalization, cultural and racial discrimination, and particularly poverty.”
They also add: “Today the state university resumes quality actions obvi‑
ously required by licensing and accreditation, to strengthen measurement 
requirements, as well as those for analysis and improvement of projects 
under the six sigma methodology, which is consistent with this article since it 
is proposed to promote reflection on the processes of improvement of educa‑
tional quality that today’s society demands at all levels of education, as well 
as providing equal opportunities for all students in the country to achieve 
comprehensive training; in cognitive aspects, in the development of abilities 
and skills, as well as their attitude for intellectual and personal growth.”
15.2.2  Fuzzy logic
Fuzzy sets theory was published for the first time in the paper “Fuzzy Sets” 
authored by the Engineer and Mathematician Lofti Zadeh in “Information 
and Control” in 1965. A fuzzy set is a class of objects with a continuum of 
degrees of membership. Such a set is characterized by having a membership 
function (characteristic), which assigns to each object a degree of member‑
ship in the range from 0 to 1.

15.2.3  Ishikawa diagram
To construct the Ishikawa diagram, the following steps must be followed:
	
1.	Definition of the problem or effect.
	
2.	Elaboration of the graphic representation, where there is a central line 
that ends in a “head” where the problem or effect is written.
	
3.	The categories or factors that influence the problem are written. They 
extend obliquely to the center line.
	
4.	Part of the process is a proactive joint determination of the causes 
affecting the problem by the designated members. The team that 
comes together can use brainstorming to fill in the chart. The causes 
are represented by lines that point the factor which they affect.
	
5.	Once the diagram has been prepared, the information that it offers is 
analyzed, the main factors and causes are highlighted as well as the 
way to modify them to solve the problem.
	
6.	This analysis should end with the preparation of a plan to make the 
necessary improvements in the causes to solve the problem.
A generic Ishikawa Diagram can be seen in Figure 15.4. Note that the 
arrows represent causes that influence other causes.
15.3  FUZZY ISHIKAWA DIAGRAM
It is necessary to make it clear that as in the original diagram, in this case, 
it is necessary to follow an algorithm to obtain the results, and the same 
procedure must be followed, except that in this new conception, we achieve 
quantitative evaluations of each cause of the influence.

Next, we will illustrate the proposed method with an example.
Example 15.1
Suppose we want to analyze the reasons for low educational quality in 
a specific generic Peruvian region.
The factors to consider are the following:
F1: Relevance: It is the factor that indicates the objective of educa‑
tion, and whom it serves. Ideally, it should respond to the needs 
of society. The results must be the formation of individuals who 
enrich society in every way, and who are capable to solve social 

A fuzzy Ishikawa diagram  299
problems, ethically and creatively. Citizens who are educated 
must be transforming positively for a better future.
F2: Effectiveness: Education must give positive results, for this it 
must have pedagogical programs, teaching methods, and faculty, 
among other aspects so that the results of education meet the 
objectives set.
F3: Pertinence: Education must be time for all members of society 
who need to study, it must be a boost to personal and collec‑
tive development, and it must be a support for every aspect of 
social life.
F4: Efficiency: Education needs material and financial resources. 
Only the necessary economic resources must be used to obtain 
the desired results, waste must be avoided because this is also 
counterproductive for society.
F5: Equity: All students must be important for education; there 
must be no difference between each one of them, except for their 
school performance. Education is important for everyone, not 
just for those economically dominant sectors. There should be no 
discrimination, based on ethnical origin, racial, gender, sexual 
preference, or economic level.
Figure 15.5  Flowchart of the proposed algorithm.

300  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
The second step is to determine and discuss the causes that influence 
each of the five factors above. For example, the equity factor is influ‑
enced by the culture of the country where the analysis is made, depend‑
ing on the degree of gender equity or racial equity existing in that 
society, which will be reflected in the causes.
Figure 15.6 shows the proposed Ishikawa Diagram.
Suppose that the weight of each factor in the posed problem is dis‑
cussed in the quality control group, and they are the following:
F1: Relevance: 4,
F2: Effectiveness: 8,
F3: Pertinence: 3,
F4: Efficiency: 9
F5: Equity: 10.
The normalized values are the following:
F1: Relevance: ω = 0.117647

2

3

F4: Efficiency: ω = 0.264706
4

F5: Equity: ω = 0.294118
5
.
Next, the causes that influence each of the factors are determined, see 
Figure 15.7.
C11: The graduate students are socially recognized by their ethical 
behavior,
C12: The graduate students are socially recognized by their 
competencies,
Figure 15.6  Proposed Ishikawa diagram on the factors that influence poor quality educa-

algorithm.
From Table 15.1, it can be seen that the fourth factor “Efficiency” 
is the one that most influences the low educational quality in the 
region, with a value of 0.6. The equity factor is the least influential 
with 0.22656. The other three are slightly bigger than 0.4, as well as 
the quality rating. That is why an economic analysis of efficiency in 
educational institutions should be done. Especially in the cause “the 
institutions are economically sustainable.”

302  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
15.4  CONCLUSIONS
This chapter was devoted to introducing a Fuzzy Ishikawa diagram, which 
is applied to measure the quality of education. The classic Ishikawa dia‑
gram is a simple and useful tool, which is why it is widely used in the indus‑
try for quality control; in addition, it has been extended to other fields, 
such as educational quality control. However, the classical model is not 
used to perform quantitative or qualitative measurements according to any 
measurement scale that allows comparing the progress made in solving the 
problem. The hybridization of FL with the Ishikawa diagram allows us 
to measure the current state of the problem, the model obtained is simple 
enough, it is feasible to automate, and can be linked to linguistic tools that 
are associated with FL. The use of this tool was illustrated with an example.

Chapter 16
A novel integrated FCM/MCDM 
methodology for evaluating 
logistic performance index
N. Yapıcı Pehlivan
16.1  INTRODUCTION
Logistics is the function responsible for the flow of materials from sup‑
pliers into an organization, through operations within the organization, 
and then out to customers (Waters, 2003). There are five key elements of 
logistics: transport, warehousing, inventory, packaging, and information 
processing (Islam et al., 2013). Various definitions of logistics have been 
existed. According to Christopher (2011), logistics is the process of stra‑
tegically managing the procurement, movement and storage of materials, 
parts and finished inventory (and the related information flows) through 
the organization and its marketing channels in such a way that current and 
future profitability are maximized through the cost‑effective fulfillment of 
orders. The glossary of Council of Supply Chain Management Professionals 
(CSCMP, 2013) defined logistics as the process of planning, implementing, 
and controlling procedures for the efficient and effective transportation and 
storage of goods including services, and related information from the point 
of origin to the point of consumption for the purpose of conforming to 
customer requirements. Council of Logistics Management defined logistics 
as that part of the supply chain process that plans, implements, and con‑
trols the efficient, effective forward and reverse, flow and storage for goods, 
service, and related information, between the point of origin and the point 
of consumption to meet customer’s requirements. Logistics explained by 
Mangan et al. (2008) includes getting in the right way, the right product, in 
the right quantity and right quality, in the right place at the right time, for 
the right customer at the right cost.
The logistics performance index (LPI) is a multidimensional evaluation of 
the logistics performance of the countries on the basis of six indicators: cus‑
toms, infrastructure, quality of logistics services, timeliness, ease of arrang‑
ing shipments and tracking & tracing. It provides the most comprehensive 
international comparison tool to assess the trade and transport facilitation 
friendliness of countries (Ojala and Çelebi, 2015).
There are a limited number of studies using the LPI dataset that focus 
on the logistics performance of the countries. Behar and Manner (2008) 

306  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
investigated the relationships between bilateral exports and logistics by 
using the LPI. Hoekman and Nicita (2011) reviewed some indices of trade 
restrictiveness and trade facilitation and compared the trade effect of dif‑
ferent types of trade restrictions applied at the border with the effects of 
domestic policies influencing trade costs. In the study, a gravity regres‑
sion‑based analysis showed that tariffs and non‑tariff measures continue 
to be a significant source of trade restrictiveness for low‑income countries. 
Korinek and Sourdin (2011) examined the importance of the quality of 
trade logistics using gravity models on the LPI 2010 dataset to correlate 
with bilateral trade. Further, the different effects of trade logistics on low‑, 
middle‑ and higher‑income countries are analyzed. Lau (2011) discussed 
the development and usage of a green LPI to compare the performance 
among industries and countries through out China and Japan.
Güner and Coşkun (2012) measured the direction of the relationship 
between LPI and different economic and social indicators, by correlation 
analysis for 26 Organization for Economic Co‑operation and Development 
(OECD) countries to determine which indicator is more decisive. Felipe 
and Kumar (2012) carried out a gravity model to determine the relation‑
ship between bilateral trade flows and trade facilitation which is measured 
through the World Bank’s LPI. Martí et al. (2014a) analyzed the effect of 
LPI components on trade in developing economies using a gravity model. 
Martí et al. (2014b), using the LPI, applied the economic approach of grav‑
ity models to identify possible patterns in the performance analysis of trade 
flows in developing countries with a maritime border. Puertas et al. (2014) 
analyzed the importance of logistics performance in the exports of EU 
countries in the period of 2005–2010 to determine the possible progress on 
behalf of the Member States. The estimations of the gravity models using 
the two‑stage Heckman model for all 26 EU countries are obtained by using 
the LPI and its components. Markovits‑Somogyi and Bokor (2014) applied 
traditional Data Envelopment Analysis (DEA) and DEA combined with 
Analytic Hierarchy Process (DEA‑PC) to evaluate the logistics efficiency 
of 29 European countries. The results of DEA and DEA‑PC are compared 
and also evaluated in light of the “logistics quality and competence” index 
of the LPI. Ojala and Çelebi (2015) presented a qualitative assessment of 
the trade and transport policy environment to analyze the country’s logis‑
tics performance with regard to components of policy affecting trade and 
logistics regulations, procedures, and operations through a case study on 
Turkey. Çemberci et al. (2015) measured the moderating effect of the global 
competitiveness index on each dimension of LPI by means of the hierar‑
chical regression method. Çakır (2017) introduced a hybrid methodology 
which is a combination of criteria importance through intercritera correla‑
tion, simple additive weighting (SAW), and Peters’ fuzzy regression meth‑
ods for measuring the logistics performance of OECD countries using the 
devised model based on the data of LPI dataset 2014. Önsel Ekici et al. 

A novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology  307
(2016) analyzed the relationship between the competitiveness indicators 
and LPI indicators using a methodology that combines artificial neural net‑
work and cumulative belief degrees to evaluate the logistics performance of 
Turkey. Mariano et al. (2017) proposed the DEA for building a composite 
index of low carbon logistic performance (LCLPI) and the window analysis 
and the Malmquist index are used to evaluate LCLPI over time. Marti et al. 
(2017) proposed a DEA approach to compute a synthetic index of overall 
logistics performance (DEA‑LPI) and benchmark the logistics performance 
of the countries with LPI. They also analyzed the potential differences 
observed when using different variables, namely income and geographical 
area. Roy et al. (2018) suggested a two‑stage methodology for the min‑
ing of the LPI 2014 dataset. At first, the LPI dataset is clustered into finite 
clusters using K‑means algorithm. Afterwards, the compatibility of mul‑
tivariate adaptive regression spline‑based regression is performed to cap‑
ture the complex non‑linear relationship between the variables. Öner and 
Öztayşi (2018) introduced a retailer segmentation approach based on fuzzy 
c‑means clustering (FCM) clustering and interval type 2 hesitant multi‑cri‑
teria decision‑making (MCDM). Chen et al. (2018) proposed an ordered 
K‑means clustering algorithm considering the preference degree between 
any two alternatives. To measure the closeness of alternatives different from 
the K‑means clustering algorithm, the relative net flow of PROMETHEE 
is applied concerning the human development indexes. Chai et al. (2019) 
is used partial correlation analysis and probit regression to form a credit 
rating indicator system composed of several indicators. Then, a technique 
for order preference by similarity to ideal solution (TOPSIS) together with 
fuzzy c‑means is applied for scoring the credit ratings of small wholesale 
and retail enterprises in a regional commercial bank in China. Bai et al. 
(2014) introduced an ordered FCM clustering algorithm to search for the 
best c‑ordered partition and obtain acceptable clustering results based on 
the FCM in coordination with PROMETHEE concerning human develop‑
ment indexes.
LPI has been used to compare the countries, regions, and income groups 
and it is an effective measure that can assist countries in identifying chal‑
lenges and opportunities and improving their logistics performance. As 
far as we know, there is no study that combines the FCM algorithm and 
MCDM methods in the literature to evaluate the logistic performance of 
the countries via the LPI index. The unique contribution of this study is 
to assess and compare the logistic performance of countries, taking into 
account the LPI and its indicators, and the gross domestic product (GDP) 
of the countries in terms of both income and geographical area. The results 
obtained by FCM/MCDM methodology are important in terms of being 
compatible with LPI 2018 ranking results.
The remainder of this study begins with a detailed review of the LPI 
in Section 16.2. In Section 16.3, after explaining the FCM algorithm and 

308  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
MCDM methods, an integrated methodology based on FCM and MCDM 
is proposed. In Section 16.4, a numerical example is carried out through the 
2018 LPI dataset to explain the proposed methodology. In Section 16.5, the 
ranking results of the proposed methodology are compared with the results 
obtained from other methods.
16.2  LOGISTIC PERFORMANCE INDEX
Logistics and transportation play an important role in international trade 
(Martí et al. 2014a). Logistics can be described as the process of planning, 
implementing, and managing the movement and storage of raw materials, 
work‑in‑progress inventory, finished goods, and the associated information 
from the point of origin to the point of consumption (Rushton and Walker, 
2007). The aim of logistics is to achieve high customer satisfaction through 
a high‑quality service with low or acceptable costs (Waters, 2003). Due to 
globalization and increased competitiveness, logistics become one of the 
most important factors for the development of international trade. Over 
the years, companies have signed many international trade agreements and 
established various organizations, such as the General Agreement on Tariffs 
and Trade (GATT), the OECD, and the World Trade Organization (WTO) 
(Waters, 2003). According to the WTO, trade facilitation is defined as the 
simplification and harmonization of the international trade procedure for 
the collection, presentation, communications, and processing of data neces‑
sary for the transportation of goods in international trade. Evaluations of 
trade facilitation have been realized through the LPI (Martí et al. 2014a).
LPI is an effective measure to evaluate the countries’ logistics performance 
according to six indicators. It is used to compare the countries, regions, and 
income groups. The LPI has been released by the World Bank from 2007 
for every 2 years is an effective measure for evaluating the logistics per‑
formance of the countries (Yu and Hsiao, 2016). It is used to compare the 
countries, regions, and income groups and also can help countries identify 
challenges and opportunities and improve their logistics performance. The 
LPI was first published in a report named “connecting to compete” in 2007 
which was conducted on 150 countries and then repeated in 2010 and 2012 
for 155 countries, and in 2014, 2016, and 2018 for 160 countries. In the 
reports, the LPI has analyzed the countries based on a worldwide survey 
through six indicators explained as follows:
Customs: The efficiency of customs and border clearance in terms of 
speed, simplicity, and predictability of formalities.
Infrastructure: The quality of trade and transport infrastructure (e.g., 
ports, railroads, information technology).
Quality of Logistics Services: The competence and quality of logistics 
services (e.g. trucking, forwarding, and customs brokerage).

A novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology  309
Timeliness: The timeliness of shipments in reaching their destination 
within scheduled or expected delivery times.
Ease of Arranging Shipments: The ease of arranging competitively priced 
shipments.
Tracking and Tracing: The ability to track and trace consignments.
The LPI is generated from these six indicators using Principal Component 
Analysis (PCA) which is a statistical method to reduce the dimensionality 
of a dataset. The LPI is performed through a web‑based questionnaire con‑
sisting of international part and domestic parts. Each respondent evaluated 
qualitative data by using a 5‑point scale between 1 (worst) and 5 (best). 
The inputs for the PCA are considered as country scores obtained by taking 
a geometric average over all respondents’ data. The PCA is applied after 
normalizing the country scores. Finally, the LPI of each country which is 
the output of PCA is computed as weighted average scores. The weights of 
the indicators are considered as customs (0.4072), infrastructure (0.4130), 
international shipments (0.3961), logistics quality & competence (0.4166), 
tracking & tracing (0.4106), and timeliness (0.4056). According to the LPI 
scores, countries are divided into four categories: logistics‑unfriendly, par‑
tial performers, consistent performers, and logistics‑friendly (Arvis et al., 
2007, 2010, 2012, 2014, 2016, 2018).
16.3  PROPOSED METHODOLOGY
The methodology consists of three stages which integrates the FCM algo‑
rithm and MCDM methods is introduced in this study. In the first stage, the 
dataset is clustered into a predetermined number of clusters. So, the member‑
ship values belonging to each cluster of the data and centroids of the clusters 
are determined. In the second stage, the ranking of the clusters is determined 
using the MCDM methods based on cluster centers (centroids) of the con‑
sidered criteria. In the third stage, the MCDM method is used to sort the 
countries assigned to the clusters according to the criteria considered.
MCDM methods have been used to solve real‑world problems that con‑
tain multiple and usually conflicting criteria considering decision‑makers’ 
judgments. A typical MCDM contains three steps: (i) determination of the 
criteria and alternatives, (ii) evaluations of each criteria and alternatives, 
and (iii) ranking of the alternatives. Selection of the MCDM methods is 
based on the nature of the problem, types of choices, measurement scales, 
dependency among the criteria, type of uncertainty, expectations, and 
qualitative/quantitative evaluations of decision makers (Hwang and Yoon, 
1981; Hatami‑Marbini et al., 2013).
In this section, the FCM algorithm is explained and then considered 
MCDM methods, i.e. TOPSIS, multi‑objective optimization by ratio analy‑
sis (MOORA), additive ratio assessment (ARAS), and SAW are briefly given.

310  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets

Each element is assigned to a cluster with a membership value between 
0 and 1 by the FCM algorithm. The higher membership values of an ele‑
ment indicate the higher strength of the association between that element 
and a particular cluster. We assign an element to that cluster with which it 
has the highest membership value. In the FCM algorithm, two important 
information, the number of clusters (c) and the order of fuzziness (m), are 
determined by the user in advance. Since the selection of (c, m) and the 
initial values of the membership values/centroids provided by the user are 
subjective, these selections influence the quality of the optimal solution.

16.4  NUMERICAL EXAMPLE
The LPI is an effective measure to evaluate the countries’ logistics perfor‑
mance. It is used to compare the countries, regions, and income groups and 
also can help countries identify challenges and opportunities and improve 
their logistics performance. In this study, dataset that include the overall 
LPI score and six indicators of the LPI as well as per capita GDP for all the 
countries, are considered. The GDP is an important measure of the growth 
of economies and at a country level, it is described as “consumption plus 
investment plus exports minus imports.”
In the proposed methodology, at the first stage, the LPI 2018 dataset is 
clustered into four income groups (high, upper middle, lower middle and 
low) on the basis of the overall LPI score and the per capita GDP of the 
countries by the FCM algorithm. In the FCM algorithm, the number of 
clusters (c) is taken as 4, and the order of fuzziness (m) is taken as 2. The 
membership value of each country to each particular cluster is determined. 
A country is assigned to a cluster for which it has the maximum member‑
ship value. For the Philippines, membership degrees belonging to Clusters 
1‑4 are 79.67%, 10.63%, 8.64% and 1.07%, respectively. Thus, the maxi‑
mum value of the membership degrees indicates the cluster to which the 
Philippines belong to Cluster 1. Turkey is assigned to Cluster 2, as the 
membership degrees belonging to Clusters 1‑4 are 17.04%, 76.44%, 4.65% 
and 1.84%, respectively. The membership degrees for Afghanistan belong‑
ing to Clusters 1–4 are 15.80%, 4.85%, 77.76% and 1.59%, respectively. 
So, Afghanistan is assigned to Cluster 3. In a similar manner, Germany is 
assigned to Cluster 4 because the membership degrees belonging to Clusters 
1‑ 4 are 3.54%, 8.18%, 2.44% and 85.84%, respectively,

A novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology  315
In the second stage, the ranking of the clusters is determined by using con‑
sidered MCDM methods which are TOPSIS, SAW, ARAS and MOORA, 
based on cluster centers (centroids) of the overall LPI score and per capita 
GDP. For instance, Table 16.1 shows the positive ideal solution, negative 
ideal solution, and closeness coefficients computed by the TOPSIS method 
for each cluster. The ranking of clusters is obtained as Cluster 4, followed 
by Cluster 2, Cluster 1 and Cluster 3 and they are identified as high income, 
upper‑middle income, lower‑middle income, and low income. Same ranking 
results were also obtained using methods of SAW, ARAS, and MOORA.
According to the income group classification by FCM/MCDM method‑
ology, Turkey is assigned to Cluster 2, i.e. “upper middle income.” This 
is consistent with the income classification of the World Bank. Although 
the FCM/MCDM classification for many countries is compatible with the 
World Bank’s classification, some are not. In particular, there is high com‑
pliance in countries assigned to high‑income and low‑income groups, while 
lower‑middle and upper‑middle‑income countries are not well aligned with 
World Bank’s classification. For instance, some of the high‑income coun‑
tries by World Bank classification (e.g. Bahrain, Brunei Darussalam, Chile, 
Croatia, Cyprus, Czech Republic, Estonia, Greece, Korea Rep., Kuwait, 
Lithuania, Malta, Panama, Poland, Portugal, Saudi Arabia, Slovak Rep., 
Slovenia and Taiwan) were identified as upper‑middle‑income group, some 
of them (e.g. Argentina, Bahamas, Latvia, and Uruguay) are assigned to 
lower‑middle‑income group and only one country, Trinidad and Tobago, is 
assigned to low‑income group according to the FCM/MCDM methodology.
In the third stage, the TOPSIS method is again used to determine the 
ranking of the countries within each of the income groups (high, upper mid‑
dle, lower middle, and low) according to the six indicators of the LPI. The 
ranking of the countries in those income groups by proposed FCM/MCDM 
and LPI 2018 constituted by the World Bank as well as MCDM methods on 
the basis of LPI’s six indicators (MCDM 1) are given in Tables 16.2–16.5. 
In these tables, ranking results obtained from the MCDM methods on the 
basis of LPI’s six indicators (MCDM 1) as well as the MCDM methods 
based on overall LPI score and per capita GDP (MCDM 2) are shown.
As shown in Tables 16.2–16.5, in terms of income groups constituted 
by the FCM algorithm, ranking results of the FCM/MCDM are generally

A novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology  321
Analyzing the ranking results of the FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018, income 
groups and geographical regions have an impact on the logistic perfor‑
mance of the countries. According to the World Banks’s classification, 
there are four income groups (high, upper middle, lower middle, and low) 
and seven geographical regions: East Asia & Pacific, Europe & Central 
Asia, Latin America & Caribbean, Middle East & North Africa, North 
America, South Asia, and Sub‑Saharan Africa. To investigate these situa‑
tions, one‑way analysis of variance for nonparametric data, Kruskal–Wallis 
H test, is used because of non‑normality of the LPI 2018 dataset. The null 
hypothesis used for this test is that the average performance is independent 
from income or geographical region. A summary of Kruskal–Wallis H test 
for income and geographical region is given in Table 16.7.
As shown in Table 16.7, the null hypothesis is rejected at a 5% significance 
level since the p‑value <0.001 for the FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018. It means 
that at least the logistic performance in some group of the countries is sig‑
nificantly different from other groups. Dunn’s post hoc tests are carried out 
on each pair of income groups and geographical regions to determine which

groups are different from the others. When performing multiple tests, SPSS 
makes an adjustment to the p‑value. The Bonferroni adjustment is to multi‑
ply each Dunn’s p‑value by the total number of tests being carried out. The 
pairwise comparisons that show the results of the Dunn‑Bonferroni tests on 
six pairs of income groups and 21 pairs of geographical regions are given in 
Tables 16.8 and 16.9, respectively.
In Table  16.8, test statistics, significance (Sig.), and adjusted signifi‑
cance (Adj. Sig.) are shown. The Adj. Sig column makes the adjustments 
for multiple testing using the Bonferroni error correction. The p‑value for 
the test comparing the income groups of the high‑upper middle (p  <0.001), 
high‑lower middle (p  <0.001), high‑low (p   <0.001), and upper middle‑low 
(p = 0.001 < 0.05) are significant (marked with *) for both the FCM/MCDM 
and LPI 2018. There is no evidence of a difference between the other pairs 
of income groups.
It is seen that from Table 16.9, the p‑value for the test comparing the geo‑
graphical regions of the Europe & Central Asia–Latin America & Caribbean 
(p = 0.005 < 0.05 for FCM/MCDM and p = 0.006 < 0.05 for LPI 2018  ), 
Europe & Central Asia–South Asia (p = 0.022 < 0.05 for FCM/MCDM and 
p = 0.020 < 0.05 for LPI 2018), Europe & Central Asia–Sub‑Saharan Africa (p  
<0.001), and East Asia & Pacific–Sub‑Saharan Africa (p  <0.001) are signifi‑
cant (marked with *) for FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018. There is no evidence 
of a difference between the other pairs of geographical regions. Pairwise

324  Data-Driven Modelling with Fuzzy Sets
16.5  CONCLUSION
In this study, we presented an integrated methodology based on the FCM 
algorithm and MCDM methods to rank the countries in terms of logistic 
performance through the LPI 2018 dataset. Although the FCM algorithm 
and MCDM methods have been widely used in various fields, combin‑
ing FCM with MCDM methods can provide effective tools for logistic 
professionals.
It is found that ranking results obtained by the proposed methodology 
are very similar to given in the LPI 2018 report. Since the Spearman cor‑
relation coefficients between LPI 2018 and FCM/MCDM, MCDM1, and 
MCDM2 are computed as 0.997, 0.971, and 0.890, respectively. This situ‑
ation shows that the ranking results of FCM/MCDM is compatible with 
ranking results of the LPI 2018 report.
This study shows that there are striking differences between the best and 
the worst countries in ranking according to the FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018 
report. As seen from the LPI reports in 2018 and earlier, the logistic perfor‑
mances of the countries are heavily dependent on income and geographical 
area. Top 10 countries are generally high‑income countries from the year 
2007 which is the beginning of the LPI. Most of these are European coun‑
tries such as Germany, Sweden, Belgium, Austria, Netherlands, Denmark, 
Finland, and the United Kingdom and they are dominant in logistics globally 
and regionally. China, Thailand, and South Africa among the upper‑mid‑
dle‑income countries in both classifications of the FCM/MCDM and World 
Bank perform best. In addition, countries such as India, Indonesia, and 
Vietnam which take place in the lower‑middle‑income according to the 
Figure 16.1  Pairwise comparisons of (a) income groups and (b) geographical regions.

A novel integrated FCM/MCDM methodology  325
World Bank’s classification and take place in the upper‑middle‑income 
countries according to the FCM/MCDM classification, stand out as the 
best performing countries.
Similarly, countries with low income and lower‑middle income, espe‑
cially the Central African Republic, Zimbabwe, Haiti, Libya, Eritrea, Sierra 
Leone, Niger, Burundi, and Angola located in Africa, and Afghanistan 
located in Asia, take the bottom positions according to the LPI 2018 report 
and proposed methodology. These countries have faced either wars con‑
flicts, natural disasters, poverty, or political unrest.
Kruskal–Wallis H test is used because of non‑normality of LPI2018 data‑
set to analyze the ranking results of the FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018 with 
respect to income groups and geographical regions. It is determined that 
at least the logistic performance in some groups of the countries is signifi‑
cantly different from other groups for both FCM/MCDM and LPI 2018. 
Dunn’s post hoc tests are performed on each pair of income groups and geo‑
graphical regions making Bonferonni adjustment. There are significant dif‑
ferences between income groups of high‑upper middle, high‑lower middle, 
high‑low, and upper middle‑low. There are differences between geographi‑
cal regions of Europe & Central Asia–Latin America & Caribbean, Europe 
& Central Asia–South Asia, Europe & Central Asia–Sub‑Saharan Africa, 
East Asia & Pacific–Sub‑Saharan Africa.
This study can help policy‑makers of countries that are at the bottom 
in the LPI ranking, to demonstrate that they have to improve their cus‑
toms, infrastructures, quality of logistics services, timeliness and ease 
of arranging shipments and tracking & tracing. For future studies, an 
ordered K‑means clustering algorithm and ordered FCM can be performed 
to cluster a great number of data on logistics much faster. In addition, 
the use of other MCDM methods (e.g. ELECTRE, COPRAS, and GRA) 
can be attempted for more practical applications under crisp and fuzzy 
environment.
